Ben Duggan.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Jack Denver died on Talbragar when Christmas
Eve began,
And there was sorrow round the place, for Denver
Was “ a man ”;
Jack Denver’s wife bowed down her head —her
daughters’ grief was wild ;
And big Ben Duggan by the bed was sobbing like
a child.
But big Ben Duggan saddled up and galloped
fast and far
To raise tr. 3 biggest funeral e’er seen on Tal-
bragar.
By station home,
And shearing shed,
Ben Duggan cried, “ Jack Denver’s dead !
Roll up at Talbragar !”
He borrowed horses here and there and rode all
Christinas Eve,
And scarcely paused a moment’s time the mourn-
ful news to leave ;
He rode by lonely huts and farms, and when the
CJay was done
He turned his panting horse’s head and rode to
Ross’s Run.
No bushman in a single day had ridden half so
far
Since Johnson brought the doctor to his wife at
Talbragar.
By digger’s camps
Ben Duggan sped
At each he cried, “Jack Denver’s dead !
Roll up at Talbragar !”
That night he passed the humpies of the splitters
on the ridge,
And roused die bullock-drivers camped at Lallan*
faulie’s Bridge ;
And as he climbed the ridge again the moon rose
on the rise,
The soft white moonbeams glistened in the tears
that filled his eyes ;
He dashed the rebel drops away—for blinding
things they are
But ’twas his best and truest friend who died on
Talbragar.
At Blickman’s Run
Before the dawn
Ben. Duggan cried, “ Poor Denver's gone.
Roll up at Talbragar !”
At all the shanties round the place they’d heard
his horse’s tramp,
He took the track to “Wilson’s Luck ” and told
the diggers’ camp ;
But in the gorge by Deadman’s Gap the mountain
shades were black,
And there a newly-fallen tree was lying on the
track —
lie saw too late, and then he heard the swift hoof’s
sudden jar,
And big Ban. Duggan ne’er again rode home to
Talbragar.
“Tne wretch is drunk,
And Denvers dead—
A burning shame !” the people said
Next day at Talbragar.
For thirty miles round Talbragar the boys rolled
up in strength,
And Denver had a funeral a good long mile in
length ;
Round Denver’s grave that Christmas day, rough
bushmen’’* eyes were dim.
The western bushmen know the way to bury dead
like him ;
But some returning homewards found, by light of
moon and star,
Ben Duggan dying in the rocks, five miles from
Talbragar.
They knelt around,
He raised his head
And faintly gasped, “Jack Denver’s dead,
Roll up at Talbragar !”
But one short hour before he died he woke and
understood,
They told him, when he asked them, that the
funeral was good ;
And then there came into his eyes a strange
victorious light,
He smiled on them in triumph, and his great soul
took its flight.
And still the careless bushmen tell by tent and
shanty bar
How Duggan raised a funeral, years back on
Talbragar.
And far and wide
When Duggan died,
The bushmen of the western side,
Rode in to Talbragar.
Sydney, Sept, ’9l.
HENRY LAWSCH.


THE HORSE-DRIVER’S COFFIN.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
When Jem, the horse-driver, meets the
Doctor he usually scowls and passes by, and when
the Doctor sees Jem in
the distance he rpostly
crosses to the other side
of the road. For there is
a coolness between them,
and it came about over
a coffin. It was not
much of a coffin, after
all. Metal-lined, clasped
mahogany boxes are not
considered indispensable
at Tellabindah (Never
Never), and this was
only a very ordinary affair
of plain hardwood, and
six feet six inches long.
It was a back-blocks
hospital coffin. But it
was the making of it and
the paying for it that led
to the trouble.
Jem was horse-driver
at Talumba, 20 miles
away. He had been 15
years in the district, with
a regular spree every six
months, which caused
the entire township to
feel a fraternal interest in
his fate, when one morning, stretched on a blan-
ket on the station dray, Jem came into the Tella-
bindah Hospital. Six-feet-four long, and always
thin and bilious-looking, Jem seemed more
cadaverous than usual this morning. It was
fever, and, as he held an impromptu levee of the
leading residents in front of the Diggers’ Arms
where the dray had made a temporary stoppage to
refresh man and beast, the opinion was freely
expressed that it was “as near as a toucher with
poor Jem this time.”
There were three patients in the hospital when
Jem arrived. They had not then and had never
had anything particular the matter with them,
but the view from the verandah was rather fine,
and it was pleasant to be able to propound their
symptoms at length once a day, and to be visited
by the committee and asked if there were any
complaints, and work was rather scarce—not that
they had any particular desire for it. So they
were resting for a while in a land where it seemed
always afternoon. They viewed the arrival of the
dray with languid interest, doubting whether this
might not lead to sitting up at night with the sick
man, and also indulging in speculations as to a
probable greater liberality in the dispensing of
medical comforts.
To the Doctor the matter was more important,
for he had not had a really sick patient for three
months, and some ill-natured people had even
gone so far as to insinuate that it was no use
paying him a salary to look after three convalescent
sundowners. This touched the Doctor on the
raw—to wit, his pocket, so when, accompanied
by the king of Tellabindah, who was also the
local storekeeper, likewise a J.P., and whose name
was Murphy, he visited Jem, he pronounced the
ease to be one of great danger, indeed.
Now, this was not wise of the Doctor, for
though Jem looked ill, he was not really so ill
as he looked. He always looked ill. But the
Doctor did not know this. He was a foreign
gentleman, with a foreign diploma, which his
enemies asserted he had obtained by garotting
the rightful owner. This, of course, in a whisper,
for the Doctor was not only a choleric man,
but a very strong man, with outlandish but
effective methods of personal combat, as a
customer who declined to part with the usual
professional fee discovered to his cost. But
having formally given his opinion he considered
the matter settled, and it was generally under-
stood that Jem was going to “pass in his cheques.”
But Jem took a different view of the matter,
and held forth to Dennis Flaherty (Dennis was
one of the patients alluded to and fetched Jem’s
tobacco on the sharing system.)
“ I had this ’ere blessed fever more nor 15 years
ago on the Palmer, and a dashed sight worse, too.
This little touch ain't a goin’ to kill me. If the
old cove ’ud only leave off punchin’ an’ proddin’
an’ examinin’ of me I’d bs all right by now,” and
Dennis who was nothing if not sympathetic said :
“ Shure, an’ you’ll be all roight directly, Jem.”
Jem was on plenty of quinine with a strictly
milk diet, which, together with the lying in bed,
made him look thinner and more cadaverous than
ever, so the Doctor thought he was worse, and
said one morning that he was sure he could not
live many days longer, which being repeated
generally caused a pleasurable thrill of excitement
through the township.
There is not a medical man living who does not
take it as a personal insult when a patient, who is
ordered to recover, dies, or vice versa, and when
the Doctor had, on several distinct occasions, pro-
nounced it impossible for Jem to live, and Jem,
although getting thinner and thinner, still refused
to fall in with the verdict, the Doctor felt himself
aggrieved, for he thought himself infallible, and
liked to be thought so too.
There was only one carpenter, who was also the
undertaker, in Tellabindah. Name, Simmons.
He had listened sympathetically to the account of
Jem’s serious state. His ordinary contract was “to
find everything and do everything at £3 10s. a
body.” But surely a friendly committee would
take into consideration the fact of a Cft. Gin. coffin
being required and make an allowance for extra
timber? Besides, Jem had taken his two sprees a
year regularly for fifteen years, which should be
an inducement to the leading residents to provide
a few extra “fittings” for such a consistent
citizen.
So one morning Simmons called on the Doctor.
“ Suppose there ain’t no chance of Jem getting
better ! ” he said.
Now, the more obstinate the patient was in
refusing to die, the more obstinate became that
foreign Doctor ’« his opinion, so he said ;
“No.”
“ Well, Doctor,” said Simmons, “ it’s very
awkward, but I’ve got to go away for a week and
if aught was to happen there’d be nobody to make
the coffin, an’ no one ’ud like to see poor Jem
buried without a coffin. Hadn't I better make it
now?"
The Doctor did not like this at all. It was
unusual and, what was worse, unprofessional.
But what could a man who had been so positive in
his opinion do ? Should no coffin be forthcoming,
they would blame him for want of foresight. So
Simmons took away the order, with a promise of
fds
“The Doctor was sure Jem would not live much longer."
secrecy.
Late in the afternoon, when the patient awoke,
Denis Flaherty was sitting beside him.
. “Jem,” he said, “do you know what that noise
is? It was the knock, knock, knock of a carpen-
ter’s hammer. “ Shure,” he continued, “ it’s that
z£=: f
“No one 'ud like to see poor Jem buried without a coffin. Hadn't I letter make it now?"
d——d thafe of a Simmons, an’ he’s building a
coffin for yez.”
Jem was ill and Jem was weak, but he sat bolt-
upright in the bed.
“ By gum !” was all he said.
“ I heard them awhiles
ago whin I was makin’
pritence to slape forninst
the verandah,” continued
Dennis, “ an’ I’ll tell
ye, thrue by the Howly
Father, unless ye clear
out av this, that ould
divil when lie gets the
coffin won't stand ye dis-
appointin’ av him much
longer."
* * #
It was after dark
when Jem cleared out of
the Tellabindah Hos-
pital, and, weak as he
was, he managed to
tramp by easy stages
back to the men’s huts at
Talumba. The coffin had
cured the fever.
# # #
At the next meeting
of the Hospital Com-
mittee a sad-eyed un-
dertaker presented, with
a doubtful air, a little
bill for “one extra-long
hardwood coffin, as per
contract, two pounds.”
The committee smiled
disdainfully and referred
him to the medical
officer, but as the med cal
officer had just shut him-
self up with his second
bottle of whisky, the un-
dertaker preferred, after
reconnoitring through
the window, to postpone
the interview.
A month later on Jem,
who was at work again,
received the following
account :
J dr. to VV. simmons.
To one extra long hard Wood
coffin for your Use, two
Pounds. To storing same Six
weaks, ten shillings.
fds
“Jem keeps the coffin as a memorial."
Jem had a sense of humour, so he paid for the
coffin, minus the storage. He keeps it as a
“memorial,” but ever since he settled the account
there has been a coolnesi between him and the
Doctor.
GROUGE DANT.
Lachesis.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Over a slow-dying lire,
Dreaming old dreams, I am sitting ;
The flames leap up and expire ;
A woman sits opposite knitting.
I’ve taken a Fate to wife ;
She knits, with a half-smile mocking,
Me, and my dreams, and my life,
All into a worsted stocking.
V.J.D.
These are true stories about Sir George
Grey : —When he was Governor at the Cape,
Sir George, following up a plan which he
had worked successfully in Maoriland, employed
the natives at road-making and paid them. The
woolly Kaffirs were so delighted with the idea of
making money that they worked, literally like
niggers, but spent their hard-earned coin in orna-
ments (of which the Kaffir ladies got most) such as
armlets, necklets, and other light finery which con-
stitutes the summer costume of the disenfranchised
native female. Now, this extravagance grieved
the heart of Grey and jarred on his
economical instincts ; and so, when he got the
Kaffir bon ton together, he made a long speech
to them, exhausting every phase of the thrifty,
saving and industrious aspects of life, and pic-
turesquely showing up the foolishness of outer
adornment. “I considered when I sat down,”
said Sir George, when he told the story, “that I
had put the case irresistibly. But, after a respectful
pause, an old chief got up and said the Governor
was a wonderful man. Ho had been in many
parts of the world, and knew a great
deal, and was so strong that he actually
got the natives to make roads through their own
land ! But he (the Chief) was a greater man than
Sir George Grey, because he was older and knew
more ; and if Sir George thought he could do any-
thing to prevent women spending their husbands’
money in ornaments and decorations, then he be-
lieved he could do what no man ever had been able
to do ! And to this day Sir George Grey retains a
lively recollection of that as the one occasion on
which he was fairly and permanently put down. In
a more recent encounter with a cultured white Sir
George was seen to better advantage. In Welling-
ton, one day, he met a member of Parliament who
knew the taste of wine when it was blood-red,
and who was sore in his domestic economy.
‘What do you think is the cause of your illness?”
asked Sir George sympathetically. “Well, you
know I’m an old man and I’ve gone through a
great deal,” said his friend. “My dear
said Sir George, with an awful closing of the right
eyelid, “do you think it’s what you’ve gone
through, or what’s g no through you ? ”
My One Admirer.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
1 watched him fading day by day
So peacefully and fair;
I saw his intellect decay—
The bloom of blight was there !
The bloom of blight was there, and I
Some dark day feared to find
Him hold his pipe in mouth awry
Sure sign of loss of mind.
And, though he praised my verse, each word
No more my heart could reach
Because I fancied there I heard
A thickness in his speech !
For I had read, with care and pain,
The Colston ease, which shows
These things mean— something on the brain !
Water, or rum—God knows.
In his Life’s glass I saw the sand
Drop swiftly, with a sigh
He was my one admirer, and
I dreaded he would die.
And if he died—this thought would pass
My lips, in gloom or glee,
Great Heavens what would become —alas !
What would become of me ?
For, when all else were cold and blind
To my poetic art,
I still could count on one warm, kind,
Appreciating heart.
And, when I’d make a joke as drear
As any epitaph,
Gods ! It would split your head to hear
His hoarse, Homeric laugh !
How oft he’s made a pilgrimage
To say —“ You are, bedam,
The greatest poet of the age ! ”
I’d murmur, “ So I am.”
Beneath my spreading mulb’ry tree,
When I’d sit down to think,
He’d lie and gaze in ecstasy—
In ecstasy and drink.
And often, too, it did occur,
When days were calm and fair,
He’d take his little porringer
Of beer, and drink it there.
Yes, he'd drink from that humble cup
Some ten times or eleven,
And, if you held your fingers up,
He’d answer—“ I am seven.
One o’ me’s here upon the ground,
And two are up the tree,
Four in some far-off vat are drowned—
And all are in D.T.”
Ah, famous wits who came by day
And night, indeed, to laugh
At Me and my Admirer, they
Remained to weep—and quaff.
Though some, too, tried, to me unknown,
To lure him past recall—
They read him poems of their own—
He spurned them, one and all !
But, if he had a fault—in fact
He had a fault, I feel ;
’Twas that his admiration smacked,
At times, too much of zeal.
I'm modest as a violet
(As all true genius is),
And shrink from fame; it is my pet
Aversion; ’twas not his.
His fancy, when with whisky fired.
Was, with a whoop, to quick
Make all admire whom he admired,
Or else—to knock them sick.
And so, while 1 concealed my name,
And all proud thoughts dismissed
With pray’r—from bar to bar my fame
He preached, with foot and fist.
And many a morn, policemen brought
Me notes like this : “ Dear Roe
I had an argument—we fought—
Up at the Fox and Crow :
“ A cove there talked, for half the night,
Of Byron, Swinburae, Scott,
I said, with calm, ‘ Creeve Roe can write
Rings round the blessed lot.’
“ He hinted that he hardly saw—
In fact, he had a doubt
I up and broke his blasted jaw—
Please come and bail me out.”
But those brave days are past! Ah, no
Days now have such a vim,
My champion's still alive, but oh,
The difference in him !
He, who could once a cask enfold,
A quart makes overflow ;
He, who could once an epic hold,
Scarce holds a small rondccm.
I tried him but last week with some
Short odes on subjects high,
But, though he was welbprimed with rum,
They knocked him into pi.
And, sometimes when he dodders round,
A wreck of rum and rhyme,
Who can’t stand half-a-pint, or pound,
Of either at a time,
I think of Besant’s Grumbelow :
When he’d the final crunch
Give his wrecks, he’d, to Ball-of-Snow,
Cry “ Bring the Noyeau Punch !”
And so, I fancy, some near day,
I’ll this poor shell explode—
By finishing him off with, say,
A new Australian Ode ;
And on his grave this scroll shall burst
On view of those who pass ;
“ Here lies my One Admirer—First
And Last—Keep off the Grass !”
CREEVE ROE.


SKITTY MCRAE
ACLLPPING
RHYME.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
1 ho western sun, ere ho sought his lair,
Skimm’d the treetops and glancing thence,
Re sted awhile on the curling hair
Of Kitty M'Crae by the bound’ry fe ncc ;
Her eyes looked anxious, her cheeks were pile,
For father was two hours late with the mail.
Never before had he been so late,
And Kitty wondered and wished him back,
Leaning athwart the big swing gate
Leading out on the bridle-track,
A tortuous path that sidled down
From the single street of a mining town.
With her raven curls and her saucy smile,
Brown eyes that glow with a changeful light,
Tenderly trembling all the while
Like a brace of stars on the breast of night,
Where could you find in the light of day
A bonnier lassie than Kitty M‘Crae ?
Born in the saddle, this girl could ride
Like the fearless queen of the silver bow,
And nothing that ever was lapped in hide
Could frighten Kitty M'Crae, I trow.
She would wheel a mob in the hour of need
If the Devil himself were in the lead.
?—■
I I m
But now in the shadows’ deepening,
When the last sun-spark has ceas’d to burn,
Afar she catches the sullen ring
Of horse-hoofs swinging around the turn,
Then painfully down the narrow trail
Comes Alec M‘Crae with the Greytown mail.
“ The fever-and-ague, my girl,” he said,
“ ’Twas all I got on that northern trip,
When it left me then I was well-nigh dead,
Has got me fast in its iron grip;
And I’d rather rot in the nearest gaol
Than ride to-night with the Greytown mail.
“ At Golden Gully they heard to-day—
’Twas a common topic about the town—
That the Mulligan Gang were around this way,
So they wouldn’t dispatch the gold-dust
down,
And Brown, the manager, said he thought
It wise to wait for a strong escort.
“ I rode the leaders, the other nags
I left with the coach at the ‘ Travellers’
Rest. ’
Kitty, my lass, you must take the bags—
Postboy, I reckon’s about the best;
Tis dark, I know, but he’ll never fail
To take you down with the Greytown mail.”
It needed no further voice to urge
This dutiful daughter to eager haste ;
She donned tire habit, of rough blue serge,
That hung in folds from her slender waist,
Postb °y stood by the stockyard rail,
While she mounted behind the Greytown mail.
Dark points, the rest of him iron-grey,
Boasting no strain of expensive blood,
Down the steepest hill he could pick his way,
And never was baulked by a winter flood—
Strong as a lion, hard as a nail,
Was the horse that carried the Grey town mail ;
C?
A nag that really seemed to be
Fit for a hundred miles at a push,
With the old Manaro pedigree,
By “ Furious Riding,” out of “ The Bush,”
Run in when a colt from a mountain mob
By Brian O’Flynn and Dusty Bob.
Old Postboy’s bosom was filled with pride
As he felt the form of his mistress sway,
In its easy grace, to his swinging stride,
As he dashed along down the narrow way.
No prettier Mercury, I’ll go bail,
Than Kitty ere carried a Guv’ment mail.
Leaving the slope of O’Connor’s Hill,
They merrily scattered the drops of dew
In the spanning of many a tiny rill,
Whose babbling waters were hid from view :
In quick-step time, to the curlew’s wail
Rode Kitty M'Crae, with the Greytown mail.
Sidling the Range, by a narrow path,
Where the tow’ring mountain ash-trees grow,
And a slip meant more than an icy bath
In the tumbling waters that foamed below ;
Through the white fog, filling each silent vale,
Rode Kitty M'Crae with the Greytown mail.
The forest shadows became less dense,
They fairly flew down the river fall,
As out from the shade of an old brush-fence
Stepped three armed men with a sudden call,
Sharp and stern came the well-known hail :
“ Stand ! for we want the Greytown mail ! ”
Postboy swerved with a mighty bound,
As an outlaw clung to his bridle rein,
A hoof-stroke flatten’d him on the ground
With a curse that was half a cry of pain,
While Kitty, trembling and rather pale,
Struck out for life and the Greytown mail.
To save the bags were her only thought
As she bent ’fore the whistle of angry lead
That follow’d the flash and the sharp report;
But “Oh, you cowards ! ” was all she said
Flying as fast as the leaden hail—
Kitty M‘Crae with the Greytown mail.
Safe ? ah, no, for a tiny stream
On Postboy’s coat left its crimson mark.
She still rode on, but ’twas in a dream,
Through lands where shadows fell drear and
dark ;
Like a wounded sea-bird before the gale
Fled Kitty M‘Crae with the Greytown mail.
And ever the erimson life-stream drips,
For every hoof-stroke a drop of blood,
From feeble fingers the bridle slips
As down the Warrigal Flat they scud,
And just where the Redbank workings lie,
She reels and falls with feeble cry.
The old horse slacken’d his racing pace
When he found the saddle his only load,
And nervously sniffed at the still, pure face
That lay upturned in the dusty road ;
Like a gathered rose in the heat of day,
She droop’d and faded, Kitty M'Crae.
Did Postboy stay by the dead girl’s side ?
Not he. Relieved of her featherweight,
He woke the echoes with measured stride,
Galloping up to the postal gate—
Blood, dust, and sweat from head to tail,
A riderless horse with the Greytown mail.
And now a river-oak, drooping, weeps
In ceaseless sorrow above the grave
On the lush-green flat where Kitty sleeps,
Hush’d by the river’s lapping wave
That ever tells to the trees the tale
Of how she rode with the Greytown mail.
"SURCINGLE."
Crow—With an “E.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Timothy Crow was a pleasant, decent old
fellow who pottered about at odd times in his
dilapidated tinsmith’s shop—a shop filled with a
litter of pots and pans and rubbish generally.
He had laid by a little money, made principally in
the early days, and on this he managed to subsist,
so what work he performed professionally was
done mainly from a love of handling the tools and
whiling away the hours. His wife was dead, and
he lived in the back room alone. Sometimes the
old woman who came to clean up things about the
place stayed awhile to talk with him, but he was
not over partial to Mrs. Button.
One day Timothy put down his tools, lifted
off the nail the green-brown coat that hung
there, and made his way down the street till
he came to the local bank. The clerk at the
counter knew him, and nodded kindly as the old
man said, bending across the counter, and speak,
ing in a low voice : “I want to send some money
to my son up in the Northern Territory.”
“I did not know that you had a son, Mr.
Crow,” the clerk observed, preparing to fill up a
form.
“It’s nigh ten years since he was down,”
Timothy answered. “He is a bit above the shop,”
he added, smiling faintly.
The clerk smiled too—to himself —and said :“ I
want your son’s name, Mr. Crow.”
“Arthur Crow,” the old man replied, and,
speaking with some little hesitation, continued •
“ put an ‘ e ’ on the end of it.”
The clerk looked up. Timothy understood, and
explained, in a genial, humble fashion : “My son
has risen a bit in the world. He is a commission-
agent up there. I haven’t seen him myself, but
some chaps that came down say that he’s a big
swell—and wears a ‘ belltopper.’ He told me him-
self in a letter not to forget the * e.’ ”
The clerk handed over the draft, and the old
man returned to the shop, took off the green-
brown coat, and went to work again on a leaky
tea-pot.
In the evening, after the departure of Mrs,
Button, who always made him uncomfortable by
her positive manner, he put the pen and ink on
the table and got out some paper and envelopes ;
arranging these before him, he unfolded the draft
and carefully read out the words “On demand
pay to the order of me, Arthur Crowe, the sum of
twenty-five pounds.” “He will be pleased to get
that, he thought, even supposing he is a bit
ashamed of his old father, and the shop—and the
Spelling of the name. I suppose it does look
better with an ‘e.’”
Thus thinking, old Timothy gradually dozed
off. Soon he awakened, and remembering
what he had to do, reached over for an envelope
and slowly and painfully scratched on it “ Mr. A.
Crow.” Then the picture of his magnificent son
came into his mind, and in the goodness of his
honest old parental heart he added “Esquir.”
The rest of the address was concluded after much
painful labour, the draft re-folded, placed in the
envelope, and sealed up. He then looked at the
letter contemplatively as it lay on the table before
him. Timothy had not intended to send any
accompanying letter of explanation with the
enclosure. It was to be a surprise as well as a
Christmas-box, and might be useful to his son in
that Territory where everything was so dear. But
later on the old man found his heart yearn after
the smart, “ gentlemanly ” young man who wore
the belltopper, so in the corner of the envelope
laboriously wrote “From his Ever Loving father,
Timothy Crow.”
A fortnight later there might have been seen
stuck in the corner of a large pier-glass over the
mantelpiece in the public room of the Territory
Hotel a letter addressed to “ Mr. A. Crow,
Esquir, commission Agent. Jumbahtown,” and in
the corner the words “FromhisEver Loving father
Timothy Crow.” It was the latter phrase’
together with the general character of the ad.
dress, that excited the grins of every visitor to the
room. Bank-manager, clerk, solicitor, auctioneer,
broker’s clerk, each one as he walked in
caught sight of the object in the corner of the
glass, went straight to it, read it attentively,
then smiled and joked about it—or said nothing
at all. There had been no malicious intent in
placing the letter where it invited so much atten.
tion, for the pier-glass was a favourite letter-rack
and saved much trouble in the delivery of corres-
pondence.
Later in the evening a well-dressed young man
with black hair, good-looking from a girl’s point
of view, and of confident style, walked into the bar.
“ Have you been in the coffee-room, Mr. Crow ?*
the barmaid enquired of him when he entered.
‘‘There’s a letter for you.”
The young man sauntered into the public room.
Several people were there, all of whom he knew. So,
nodding around to the company, he walked to the
glass, took the envelope and read the address and
message scribbled in the corner, noting especially
the illiterate character of the production. Simul-
taneously there flashed across his mind a mental
procession of curious visitors to the room, who had
examined the letter and commented on it de-
risively. Thus thinking, he felt thoroughly sick
and disgusted and put the letter back in its place
unopened.
“That’s for you, Crow, isn’t it?” the bank-
manager inquired blandly from the table.
“ That thing !” and the young man inclined his
head contemptuously towards the pier-glass.
“No, thank you, dear boy, my name is spelt with
an ‘e.’ I don’t know who on »arth Timothy
Crow is. He is certainly not my father ! ’
As he finished speaking the proprietor of the
“Territory” entered the room. “Oh, you got
your letter, then, Mr. Crow,” he remarked cheer-
fully, observing the young man in front of the
?iass.
“That’s not for me, damn you!” the young
man replied, out of temper; but the proprietor
took no notice of the speech or of its tone. It was a
free country, and his guests could curse and swear
at him just as they pleased so long as they ordered
drinks and paid for them.
The obnoxious envelope remained for a few days
in the corner of the pier-glass, and then was seen
no more. It had probably fallen on to the mantel-
piece, then into the fireplace, and finally been
conveyed to the rubbish-heap. Certain it is that
the young man who spelt his name with an “ e ”
did not take possession of it, and his peace of
mind became rapidly restored concurrently with
its disappearance.
The “From his father, Timothy Crow ” incident
had put a temporary check upon the smart young
man’s manner of swelling it around among “ The
Territory’s ” habitues, yet the disclaiming of his
old father’s missive caused him but little pricking
of conscience. He knew what the old man would
have to say—“l hope things are prospering with
you, my son, trade is very dull down here, things
are going from worse to bad, and Missis Button
hopes you are getting on well.” And so on. He
had received such letters before. j
Several months passed and Crow, the commis-
sion-agent with an “e” to his name was still
the most gorgeous potentate in the township
of Jumbahtown. Nobody could possibly sus-
pect the father of so distinguished-looking a
personage to be an illiterate'old tinker somewhere
down South, and even the son had almost for-
gotten it, but the grey-headed father thought
always of his son as he rummaged among the pots
and tins, and daily expected a letter from the
Northern Territory. Yet, when he found that it
came not, he did not mind ; “ so long as Arthur’s
got the draft, that’s all I care about.” It was
thus the o;d man tried to console himself.
But one evening the old fellow got out his pens
and paper, and, labouring with much zeal and
patience, at last produced the following letter
“Mi Deer Son, — I expect you got mi letter with the
draft from the bank for £25 in it, perhaps you was too'
busy to rite and say you had got it, I am going to marry
Missis Button, but if you think I ought not to marry just
rite and tell me, and I will tell her I am too old, and you
won’t have it. lam going to make my will, I will wate
till you rite first, I feel I ought to konsult you first about
the above steps, you will rite I no mi son this time.”
Again the decent old tinsmith scrawled an
address to his son up in the Territory. It was
difficult to break an ignorant scratchy pen of a
life-long habit, so the “ E ’’-less Crowe again went
on its mission.
Tiie landlord of the Territory, a week later,
observed casually to Mr. Crowe, the commission-
agent, as he entered the bar: “Another of those
letters for you, Crowe, addressed like the other,
you remember, from Timothy Crow —came to-day.
I said it was of no use to leave it, and sent it back.
I suppose that it is all right ?”
The bank-manager and solicitor, and one or two
more of the commission-agent’s friends were
standing by as the landlord spoke.
“Of course it was. It was not for me, Hurley,
old man, you knew that—come on boys, name youi
liquors,” said the commission-agent, putting
down half-a-sovereign.
No more letters arrived at the Territory Hotel
to be rejected by the thoughtful Hurley, or any-
one else, for lack of the second vowel; and the
young man whose name was Crow, “spelt with
an ‘e,’” was not again called upon to use bad
language on that account.
The months passed by, and one afternoon the
coach brought among its passengers to Jumbah-
town an elderly man who had travelled from far
down South. He had made friends with the
driver, who had taken a fancy to the honest-
spoken old fellow. The old man was going up to
see his son, “ whom he had not seen for nigh
upon ten year,” but he was a fine young man for
all that, he had heard. It gladdened him im-
mensely when the driver said “ he knew his son, if
his name was Crow.” And then the old man
became communicative and told of a Christmas-
box which he had sent his son a good while ago.
“ I did think somehow he would -"'rite and tell
me he had got it.”
The driver thought so too.
“ But I didn’t mind that till I wrote again, and
then I felt it a bit when I still got no answer.”
Tor no suspicion of postal miscarriage or any
possible cause of non-receipt by the owner had
ever entered the simple parental old head.
Now, the driver of Jumbahtown coach had
heard all about the rejected letters from Hurley
in the coach yard, on each occasion. “He is a
mean pup and liar,” Hurley had said with
righteous indignation. “ I opened the last letter
and it was from his poor old man. He is a mean
cur ! ”
Much interest was exercised at the long table of
“ The Territory ” that night by the presence of
“Crowe’s old man.” Some said that the son
would have repudiated the father as he had done
his letter save for Nature’s unmistakable evidence,
but his behaviour at table provoked no comment.
“ You are an old fool, that’s all I’ve got to say,
though you are my father.” They were sitting
in the office of Arthur Crowe, commission-agent,
an hour after dinner.
“Don’t be hard on me, Artz,” was the reply in
a penitent, humble voice. “If you had only
opened my letter ’twould have been all right.
For I would have done what you told me.”
“Damnation! couldn’t you think and act for
yourself? you’re old enough ! ” was the son’s cruel
response.
“Don’t say that, Artz, You don’t know what
she was. When she had you by yourself. . . .
It was done before I knew it and . If you had
only written, Artz, and said you objected, that
was what I wanted—l didn’t want to marry her.”
“ You did marry her, there can be no two ways
about it. And to make over your property to her !
Great God ! ”
“ She told me to go to you, Artz —said you’d be
glad to give me a home, but I’ll go back if you
like,” said the old man, sadly and meekly.
And go he did. Jumbahtown talked about it
for a day or two after, whenever it got together
in knots and conversation fell slack: but no
retributive act to fall upon the head of the com-
mission-agent was agreed upon, and Crowe was
just as much one of the boys as ever. His half-
sovereign, put down upon the counter to pay for
drinks, is still as acceptable as any other, "Not
that the moral sense in Jumbahtown is below the
average. It is because it is of the average that
Arthur Crowe does not writhe beneath the local
scorn.
This much must be said, however. Instead of
bank-drafts arriving in Jumbahtown for Crowe,
spelt with an “e,” regularremittances proceed from
Jumbahtown, destined for an old man down
South whose name also is Crow, but without an “e.”
He receives these remittances with thankfulness
and gratitude; and a hope and trust that ere
he dies he may be permitted to clasp a loving son
to his honest, simple, old heart

Li Chew’s Revenge.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Bandicoot Inn certainly did a good
oeer-trade —a trade out of all proportion to the
size of the township, which -consisted of about a
dozen buildings. But then the Bandicoot was the
only pub. for miles round, and although the
population of the district was sparse, it included
a few regular “ soakers,” whose expenditure was
simply tremendous.
Baxter, the landlord, was a little sharp man,
with a thirst that vegetables could be cooked
upon. He paid a big rent for the place, but that
didn’t matter much, because-Lushton, his land-
lord, spent the rent and more at the hotel. And
amongst other good customers were Craddick,
who received regular monthly remittances from
England, and drank
them as regularly ; and
Dableigh, a small squat-
ter, who drank out his
flocks and herds at Bax-
ter’s. Then there were
Johnson and Wrigley,
the two local store-
keepers, who conspired
to keep up high prices
during the day, and to
keep up high jinks at the
Bandicoot at night. So
if Baxter’s customers
were not very numerous
they were arid and par-
ticularly select.
Baxter was a bachelor,
and his establishment
comprised Li Chew (a
Chinese cook), and a
rouseabout who answered
to the name of Moss,
probably owing to the
resemblance of his hair
and beard to that un-
cared-for product of
Nature. On the whole
these two had not a
very happy time of it,
for Baxter, though very
obsequious to his cus-
tomers, was at times
shockingly abusive to his
underlings. Moss’s feel-
ings did not suffer much,
perhaps, for he was
always drunk, or nearly
always so, and walked
about in a partially un-
conscious state What
Li Chew felt on such
occasions could not be
gathered from his coun-
tenance —he only smiled.
One morning the
weather was wet, a,nd
business dull —a reaction
from the previous night.
Baxter felt dull, too, and
in a very bad humour.
Passing the kitchen,
therefore, he stopped and
blasphemed at the cook
on general principles,
and, as usual, Li Chew
smiled his own apologetic
smile —the same old pre-
historic smile which the
original Chinaman used
when he woke in a new
world, and said that
things were “Allee li.”
Perhaps it was the smile
that aggravated Baxter
—it is difficult to say.
Anyhow, he suddenly
clutched his Asiatic fol-
lower and rushed him
furiously up and down
the passage by the pig-
tail. When he was tired
of this exhilarating
exercise he kicked the
terrified Celestial back
into his kitchen. Then
the heathen rubbed the
afflicted parts and smiled,
but the smile was pecu-
liar. Could anyone have
interpreted it he would
have known that Li
Chew meditated an
awful vengeance.
The Mongol pottered
about the kitchen that
day just as usual, but in
the silence of the night
he sat down and com-
posed a strange-looking
epistle to a Melbourne
cousin, asking him to
obtain and send up to
him a specified quantity
of a certain drug.
A few days afterwards
there was an unusually
large number to dinner,
and before serving it Li
Chew locked his kitchen-
door and steadily mixed
a mysterious-lookinggrey
powder with the soup
and other enticing con-
coctions And his smile
as he did so was the
smile of a fiend boiling
a martyr in a cauldron.
# # #
Li Chew continued
to perform the mys-
terious rite witli the grey powder for some time,
and soon there was an unaccountable falling-off in
trade at the Bandicoot. One forenoon Baxter sat
on his verandah smoking, and wondering what
had become of the local thirst. Most of his
regular customers had not come near him for
days. He sat and looked very dismal and swore
huskily, but his face brightened when he saw
Craddick and Dableigh, two of his surest sources
of income,riding into the township, and he strolled
into the bar to be ready to serve thein. But,
contrary to all precedent, they did not turn into
the bar —they made their way straight to the
billiard-room, whither Baxter followed, with a
gloomy foreboding that they had sworn off, and
intended to drink no more. But his fears were
soon allayed, for the squatter said —“I’ll give you
ten out of fifty, Craddiok, for drinks.”
Craddick accepted the challenge, and the game
terminated in Dableigh’s favour. Then the drinks
were brought m—whisky for both. But instead
of drinking they sat on the table and talked. At
last Dableigh said :
“ You can take mine back again, Baxter. I
ink I’ll have an English ale instead.”
u Ditto here,” said Craddick.
So a'bottle'of ale was produced, but to the
publican’s consternation, his two best customers
raised the potions to their lips, only to set them
down again untasted.
“ I’m hanged if I care to drink at all to-day,”
observed Craddick. , .
Th at s curious. I was just about to say the
same thing,’ laughed the squatter; where s
Moss-it 11 be fun to see him put away the lot.

ing up in their usual state of consternation, and
cheerfully undertook to drink up everything. He

of anticipation. He tackled the whisky first, but
put it down without sampling it; then he tried

have them drinks later on, if it’s all the same to
you, gents ; ” and as it was all the same to them,
Craddiok paid for the untasted liquor, and they
rode away too full for speech. They had often
before ridden away from the Bandicoot “ too full
for speech,” but this time they had witnessed a
miracle. Moss had refused a drink !
Unfortunately for Baxter, all his customers
behaved in the same unaccountable way. John-
son and Wrigley, although they turned up to play
cards cr billiards, and to dinner, in the old style,
never spent anything on liquor, and it was the
same with all the others. As it wasn’t “public”
policy to keep an odd man who was too odd to
drink, the landlord sacked Moss and procured
another old soaker, who could and would drink,
but he left in a day or two because the “ bloomin’
billet wasn't worth a booze a week.” Then the
unhappy publican tried to get Lushton to reduce
the rent on the plea that trade was bad, but that
worthy couldn’t see it. He was now a teetotaller,
and, so far from reducing the rent, he would have
liked to raise it so as to get back some of his much-
regretted coin from the publican.
# # #
Li Chew still remained faithful to the
fallen Bandicoot, and made daily incantations
with the mysterious grey powder, and the label on
the outside of the package read —“The Golden
Specific—a sure cure for drunkenness.” But
Baxter’s appetite for strong drink had not de-
parted like the others. Li Chew took care of
that. The publican, like many of his occupation,
was a poor eater, and cared only for one or two of
the heathen’s most tempting dishes. These Li
Chew never interfered with, and day after day the
Chinaman’s shattered enemy sat in solemn soli-
tude, and gloomily drank up his own stock, and
grew red-nosed and despondent. But Baxter still
stuck to the Bandicoot Inn, and Li Chew stuck to
Baxter, though the payment of his wages had now
become a matter of doubt. He was waiting with
the patience of his race for his revenge, which he
enjoyed to the full when the following advertise-
ment appeared in the papers :
Samuel Baxter, of Barktown, licensed victualler. Cause
of insolvency—falling off in business, high rent and
pressure of creditors. Liabilities, &c., &c.
» * •
The sign of the -Bandicoot Inn has been
altered (o “Barktown Coffee Palace, Li Chew,
proprietor.” Li Chew has also some reputation as
a Chinese doctor, and is making money. Baxter
is a rouseabout on Dableigh’s station now, and
whenever he meets his former cook he affects not
to see him. But Li Chew doesn’t mind that. He
only smiles. He has smiled off and on ever since
that day when Baxter dragged him about in the
passage, but connoisseurs assert that the time
when his grin was most affable was that on which
his old employer went in a bloated and dejected
state down the front steps never to come back. On
that occasion he spread both his hands out and
exclaimed, in a loud voice, “ Hi-yek !” Probably
it was a Chinese blessing.
J. A. MUIRHEAD.
Large, outstanding ears have long been
regarded as emblems of honesty, ingenuousness,
and generosity in young people. Boys mostly
have these symptoms well developed, little girls—
well, not much. The sex is born artful and care-
ful, not to say stingy, as a rule. Big-eared boys,
too, are found to get rid of their honorable dis-
tinction as time goes on. With the loss of in-
genuousness the outgrowth of ear ceases, and the
sound catcher commences to curl up a trifle as th e
young man acquires a taste for money, or a strong
desire to “get on” in a world where generosity
stands in the way of a “successful career.” And
when the middle - aged
sinner forgets to be honest
his ears begin to fall back
upon his skull. Then
his parents—if they are
alive, and study physical
evidences—look at their
boy of forty-five years
standing and sigh for
the ears that are gone.
Always raise your hat to
the lad who has plates
of gristle projecting
violently from either side
of his innocent young
head. Nature, we fancy,
intended them for sugges-
tions of angels’ wings.
TOMMY'S EARS.
We used to. look at
Tommy’s ears,
My Mary Jane and I,
Our sorrow didn’t run to
tears
But often we would
sigh
To see them sticking out
so far,
Instead of keeping
coy—
You know how faddy
parents are
About an only boy.
In linen they were neatly
bound
When Tommy went to
bed—
His mother put a bandage
round
The urchin’s curly
head ;
But O! those ears ap-
peared to scorn
The pressure so ap-
plied
And stuck out worse
when, in the morn.
That bandage was
untied.
Our ears were neat
enough,indeed,
And not obtrusive
thus—
He didn’t take (we both
agreed)
Such ugly things from
us.
Young Tommy went his
joyous way,
And never seemed to
care
How far across the land-
scape they
Were stretching
through his hair.
The light of truth was
on his face,
Big merry eyes he
had,
No stain of action mean
or base
Had come upon the
lad.
He’d laugh andsing with-
out a pause,
Except it was to shout —
And yet we felt aggrieved
because
His ears were sticking
out.
Ileigho ! for those good
olden days;
There’s simple truth,
we find,
In what some cynic
student says
Concerning human-
kind.
His maxim may provoke
a sneer,
Perchance a guilty
start,}
But, ah ! “the flat, out-
standing ear
Denotes the honest
heart.”
We often look at Thomas
now,
And sadly think of
then;
And though, of course,
we won’t allow
He’s like most other
men,
Maybe we wish the cute
and quick
Had gone to join the
dead
Before his ears began to stick
So closely to his head.
Time bent them back by slow degrees
Without a linen band,
He had to make his bread and cheese,
And—well, you understand.
Did’st ever see a cunning horse —
The sort a backer fears—
A-sidling down a racing course,
And laying back his ears ?
But to our castle home in Spain
We sometimes fly in grief,
And Tom is there a boy again
With ears in bold relief ;
His mother doesn’t want them tied,
Nor calls them ugly things
They seem as emblems sanctified
Of little angels’ wings.

A COW STORY.


The Ghost’s Victory.
AN ORTHODOX CHRISTMAS STORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’ This is a tough contract,” murmured the ghost
as it gazed out of the window of the haunted
chamber and looked up and down the almost
deserted street. Needless to say it was Christmas
Eve, and the clocks had just struck twelve.
It was not at all an awe-inspiring ghost. If one
had not known for certain that it was a spectre it
might have been taken for a shabby old debt-
collector or a haggard bailiff; but it was a genuine
goblin, and anybody infringing the trade-mark
will be prosecuted.
The house it haunted was an old stone affair,
situated on a good-sized allotment in one of the
principal streets of Sydney. Most people have
passed the place and wondered why it was so dull
and murky and desolate, and why a tattered bill
always announced that the upper part was “to
let.” It was owned by an absentee landlord, and
there was a law-suit on it, and three mortgages*
Around it was a solemn, melancholy waste*
tenanted by old jam - tins and goats. Th e
ground-floor was supposed to be occupied a s
offices, whilst the upper portion was left to solitude
—and the ghost.
I his was the spectre’s grievance. Thirty years
before it had cut its throat there, and since enter'
ing into the land of spirits it had not had a single
opportunity of frightening anybody and achieving
a reputation. It had madejust one publicappear-
ance, and then it fell flat. It was an orthodox
apparition, and as such was strictly obliged to
appear only between midnight and cockcrow,
but nobody lived in the house, nobody knew it
was haunted, nobody cared whether it was
haunted or not. So there it had remained, a
poor, forlorn, neglected ghost living alone in the
dark and damp, all forgotten.
“Christmas again,” it muttered in spectral
grammar, “and still lam unknown. O! if I
were only one of these modern young ghosts who
go round visiting friends and relations and scaring
the wits out of them j but, no ! X am old-fashioned
and must abide by the rules of the game. Why, I
might have heaps of hidden money here to show
people for aught the public know.” Then it fell
to musing over the one chance that had fallen in
its way.
It had been gazing sadly out of the window as
usual at about one o’clock in the morning when
there came the sound of unsteady steps down the
street. Presently a stout old gentleman in even-
ing dress, with his hat on one side and an overcoat
on his arm, stopped suddenly in front of the gate
and gazed curiously at the building. Would he
come in? The ghost’s heart beat fast with
anxiety.
“ Astonishing !” said the old gentleman. “ How
on earth did I get home so quick? Don’t re-
member the tram—don t remember the train. My
house, though, right enough.” He opened the
crazy gate and lurched in.
“At last, at last!” cried the apparition, re-
straining itself by a mighty effort from appearing
on the verandah and prematurely flushing its
game.
The old gentleman tried to put a latch-key in
the door, but as there was no keyhole he failed.
Then he kicked and knocked and swore. Finally
lie coiled himself up on the verandah, and after a
grunt or two went fast asleep.
Then the exultant spectre passed through the
crack underneath the door, and stood contem-
plating its unconscious victim. But all the old
gentleman did was to turn over and say, pettishly,
as he dragged his coat over his shoulders, “Don’t,
Maria!”
This put the ghost on its mettle. It laid an ice-
cold hand on the sleeper and, in a thrilling
whisper, said, “Awake!”
The old gentleman sat up and blinked at the
vision of the dead, and the vision regarded the old
gentleman.
“Eight, old boy !’’ said the latter. “I’ll wake
up in time, never fear. Bring me a stiff brandy-
and-soda at 8. Good night. Gobless you !” and
he lay down again and snored.
This was maddening. A ghost to be mistaken
for a waiter and told to bring a brandy-and-soda
in the morning ! It fled through the top window
to the haunted chamber and sat down on the
bloodstain and cried.
Time passed, but no other mortal visited the
haunted house between midnight and daylight.
Other ghosts came. Ghosts who were allowed out at
nights; and they used to tel! the old deceased in-
habitant what joyous times they had ; but that
only made things worse. Sometimes it thought
that a burglar might break in during the small
hours and give it a chance ; but, then, the occu-
pants of the two alleged offices hadn’t enough
between them to allure any respectable burglar;
and the burglars knew it.
Sorrowfully the poor old apparition mused, and
if it could only have repeated the operation of
cutting its throat it would have done so out of
sheer desperation and ennui.
At that moment the sound of approaching foot'
steps broke the stillness.
Two men, in earnest conversation, halted oppo-
site the gate, and, by the light of the street lamp,
the ghost saw that one of them was the occupant
of one of the offices. He was some sort of an
agent—general commission- and, as the ghost
know, his principal occupation was hiding behind
some old tanks in the back-yard when his creditors
called, which they did constantly. The old ghost,
in its invisible state, used to do a good deal of
wandering about in the day-time.
After a few minutes’ more talk the two men
came in, and the agent proceeded to unlock the
door.
The ghost nearly shrieked with joy. How
should it make the most startling appearance? It
decided to await them in the passage and shake
its gaunt arms in the shadows.
.The agent struck a match, opened the door of
his office, and was about entering, followed by his
companion, when, by the expiring light of the
match, the latter caught a glimpse of the appari-
tion standing at the end of the passage,
“ Hallo! somebody living here?” he said.
“No,” said the agent, who had lit another
match and was searching for a candle.
“Thought I saw sometliing at the end of the
passage.”
“Pooh! Oats, I suppose. This is a regular
meeting-place of theirs ;” and the agent picked up
an empty stone ink-bottle and, going to the door,
hurled it down the passage, crying “Shoo! get
out!” Then he shut the office-door.
The ink-bottle went clean through the ghost,
but the insult hurt it worse. First, to be taken
for a waiter, then to be “ shoo’ed ” at for a cat.
It approached the office vowing vengeance, and
tramping frantically with the spectres of its old
shadow feet on the floor. It was about to pass
through the door, and with a blood-curdling shriek
appear before them, with its gashed and bleeding
throat, but a few words it heard arrested it. The
ghost stopped and listened.
“Come, Tom, hurry up and get me those papers
I left with you ; I must go on board, we start at
five sharp,” said the younger man to the agent.
Tom sat on the edge of the ricketty deal table,
swinging one leg moodily to and fro, but made no
AySj7fr\LiA** s iff '"* "
