attempt to move.
“ You know how frightfully hard-up we’ve
been ?” he said at last.
“ Of course I do; has not my sister told me all
about it to-day, and, as you know, I gave her all
the money I could to keep you going for a bit.
I can do no more. Give me the papers.”
“ Well, then,” said the agent, getting up,
desperately and defiantly, “ I haven’t got them.”
“Not got them!” repeated the other. “God
Almighty, man ! what do you mean?”
“ Mean what I say.”
“What have you done with them?”cried the
younger, fiercely.
“ The bank has them, as security.”
The agent’s brother-in-law gazed at him in
silence. “Do you know,” he said at last, quite
quietly, “ that you have ruined me ?”
“ I can redeem them, and send them to you
overland,” muttered the other, with white lips.
“ Bah ! you redeem them; how?”and he glanced
round the poverty-stricken room. “Even if you
had the money, or could get it, how could you
redeem them before Monday? This is Friday,
and before you could forward them we should
have left Brisbane. You’ve ruined yourself, and
now, ruined me.”
The agent said nothing, and his companion
walked impatiently up and down the room.
“ How much did you get advanced on them ’’’
“ Five hundred pounds.”
“ And what became of it?”
“ Speculated with it. That was the temptation.
I knew of such a safe thing.”
“ Such a safe thing !”said the other, mockingly.
There was silence for a time. The younger man
continued his wild-beast walk, and the agent
leant against the table with folded arms and eyes
cast down.
Half-an-hour passed, but the ghost was not
impatient; after thirty years of loneliness, this
little tragedy was interesting. Besides, it had
cut its throat for doing almost the same thing
the agent had done. Would the agent cut his
throat ? In that case he would also become a
ghost.
“ I dare not go back with ut those deeds,” said
the young man, at last. “How do you propose
to get them back ?”
“ I think I can borrow the money.”
“ If that is all I have to rely on, why, they are
gone. Well, if it comes to the worst, I must go
to the bank and demand their restitution as stolen
property. I don’t know how the law stands, but
I believe they will give them up and ”
“ Prosecute me,” said the agon* - .
“ I suppose so. I will do my duty even though
it puts you in gaol.”
“You will put me in gaol, will you ?” snarled
the agent, walking up to his companion with an
ugly look on his face.
The ghost executed a double shuffle in his joy,
and pranced about in the passage. There was
going to be a murder, and he would have another
ghost to bear him company.
The young man confronted his brother-in-law,
and said, quietly, “If every other means fails, I
must inform the bank ; and after that I cannot
interfere. ”
Tom fell back, and said, after a pause, “You
had better leave me. I must try and think of
some way out of this.”
The other seemed in no hurry to stay, and
walked to the door, merely remarking, “I will see
you this afternoon.” Then the front-door
slammed behind him, and the house was left to the
agent and the ghost.
Was the agent going to cut his throat? The
ghost looked anxiously in; such an opportunity
was worth waiting thirty years for.
Was he going to cut his throat? Nothing of
the sort. The agent looked quite relieved, as
though an unpleasant task had at last been got
over. He lit another candle and turned towards
the old-fashioned fireplace on one side of the
room. The ghost watched him curiously. It was
a hearth for burning wood ; and the agent prized
up one of the side stones, and from a hole under-
neath took out a large cash-box, put it on the table,
and opened it. The spirit rushed through the
wall unseen, and looked over his shoulder. The
box was full of sovereigns. The agent counted
them over with a sigh of relief, replaced the
box in its hiding place, and made ready to go.
The apparition had passed through the wall
again into the dark passage. It would never do to
scare the agent now ; he was coming back for that
box, and then—the ghost hugged itself in antici-
pation. The agent closed and locked his door;
slammed the front-door after him and departed.
The ghost stopped behind and chuckled. It was
all as plain as the proverbial pikestaff, and to be
summed up in one word, “bolt.” The five
hundred pounds had not gone in speculation ; they
were in the cash-box, besides other little pickings.
The spectre reckoned it all up on its shadowy
fingers; holiday time, crowds travelling about,
best chance in the world to get away ; he’s off,
with whatever he can lay his hands on, leaves his
wife and children to do the best they can, robs his
brother-in-law, and ruins the people who own
those deeds ; now, how can I circumvent him ?
The ghost fell into a brown study. “If I could
only materialise myself like some of these new
fungled spirits say they can I might collar that
money and tell the young fellow all about it, but
I can t. If I appear before him I shall scare him,
and then he might not come back here. I’ll best
that fellow though if I ” —die for it, the ghost was
about to say, but as he had done that already ho
changed the expression. Just then the daylight
lightened the eastern sky, and it vanished, but its
face wore a look of satisfaction that was not on it
before ; evidently the apparition had an idea. t
Christmas day passed in the usual manner, so
far as the ghost was concerned ; that is to say,
nobody came near the place, and it either sat on
the blood-spot or looked cut of the window. Mid-
night struck, and the ghost was happy. It had
six hours of visibility before it, and in a shadowy
sort of way it cleared the deck for action.
At half-past twelve the agent made his appear-
ance carrying a small portmanteau.
He struck a match before closing the front door,
and dropped it immediately with a yell. Some-
body was leaning against the door of his room, a
strange, mouldy-looking little man who smelt
exactly like a damp umbrella. Resisting the im-
pulse to turn and fly, he waited and listened,
ihere was no sound but the violent thumping of
his own heart. He looked round into the street
behind him to reassure himself, mustered up his
nerve, and struck another match. This time the
passage was empty. He wiped the beads of per-
spiration from his forehead and laughed with
considerable difficulty. Then he unlocked his own
door, but he left the front
door open. Nobody in his
room so far as he could see
with a mat’ll, so he lit the
candle and —it was no fancy.
An old man with a ghastly
white face and a horrible
blood - stain on his white
beard and shirt front was
sitting 100.-e in the air about
three feet above the floor.
He might have stood Ids
ground even then, but the
thing started to walkthrough
the air towards him, and,
smitten with unreasoning
panic, he turned and ran
through the open door, and
the triumphant ghost
heard his flying footsteps
die away in the distance.
“ Now for the other man,”
muttered the ghost.
One o’clock struck, and
in a few minutes the ghost
heard someone entering the
house. Both doors were still
open and the candle still
burning, so the new-comer
walked straight in He
stopped short inside the
room. Seated at the table
where he expected to find
his relative was a seedy old
gentleman who, in spite of
the heat, had his coat
buttoned right to the neck
and his collar turned up.
The visitor was silent with
astonishment, but thespectre
grinned at him in an
amiable manner and he
faltered out at last :
“Who are you, and what
are you doing here ?”
“So glad you spoke,”
said the apparition, blandly.
“ You see I’m a ghost and,
of course, I can’t speak first.
Not allowed by the regula-
tions. Now, don’t be fright-
ened and run away, because
I want to do you a good
turn. How do you feel?
Quite well ?”
“You’re a ghost! But I
don’t believe in them. It’s a
joke.”
“Don’t be sceptical, young
man. Shall I walk through
the table or do any little
absurdity of that sort ? But,
no! you’ve more sense than
that. Listen ; Your brother-
in-law is a thief, you know,
but he is worse than you
think. He did not lose the
money the bank advanced
him. He has it all in a
cash-box under that stone.
Lift it up and see. He was
going to bolt. There is his
portmanteau.”
As in a dream, the live
man advanced and did as
the dead man directed him.
Sure enough, he lifted out a
weighty cash-box,
“If I were you,” said the
ghost, “I would go straight
to his house with that, and
make him open it in front
of your sister. His wife,
isn’t she?”
“Yes.”
“Poor woman? Well, good morning, my boy
Wish you luck.”
The young man halted at the door. “Is there
any little thing I can do, sir, to show my grati-
tude?”
“There is. Now and again, say at intervals of
a few months, persuade someone to come here
between midnight and daylight. A sceptic, if
possible. I won’t give them much of a fright.”
“Rely on me, I have half-a-dozen in my
mind’s eye already;” and he backed rather
rapidly out into the street.
“A nice young man,” said the ghost. “Now,
I wonder if that other fellow is coming back. If
ho wants to clear out before daylight he’ll face
anything to get that money.”
Sure enough, in about an hour’s time, the agent
came back. He had been somewhere in quest of
Dutch courage, and was pot-valiant. The candle
had gone out, but he lit another and glared round
the room.
“ Now, wh re are you?” he cried. “Come out
and frighten me again if you can. I’ve got some,
thing here for you.” Looking through the wall
the ghost saw that he was flourishing a revolver. ’
Cursing himself for a fool for running away
before, he put the weapon down and went to the
hearth. The cry he gave when he discovered his
loss was tremendous. He sprang to his feet, and
there, glaring at him across the table, was the old
man with the gashed throat. Then three shots
were fired in quick succession. Two of them went
through the spectre. The third went through the
agent.
There are two ghosts in that old house now.
One occupies the upper, and one the lower portion
—but they arc not on speaking terms.
DELCOMYN.


THE DEATH WATCH
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The only warning we had was Tom's tonified ex-
clamation aa he saw it coining.
The breeze had been blowing rather intermit-
tently all the time, with sudden gusts and calms
succeeded by a steady gale for about ten minutes,
and then long whispering pauses when the sails
flapped and her head shifted.
It was just after one of these short gusty blows
that there came a most extraordinary lull.
There was an absolute drop of the wind and the
sail fell flat, then there was a sort of plain head
wind more like the suction of a breakwater than
anything else I can think of, and then next
moment we all started alert at Tom’s low cry and
the whistle of the towering air-columns coming
“.full split ” upon our counter.
The four of us sitting on the ledge did what we
could at once, acting instinctively and letting go
the sail-ropes with frenzied haste.
Horsford held the tiller, and I believe that if he
hadn’t lost his head he might have saved us.
As it was I was just thinking to myself that I
would get out my knife and cut the mainsail ropes,
which had caught when we heeled right over, and
the next moment I was struggling under the boat
in the water.
When I got clear and ro-o to the surface, I
had at first nothing but an impression of the
boat upside-down, bending to one side with the
centreboard at the same angle, and the fellows
splashing about away from her.
Then I recognised Horsford just behind me,
Tom in front of me, and I could see Syd’s little
round shaven skull bobbing about a little beyond
him to the other side.
The same idea seemed to strike us all at once,
and despite the violent wind and churning sea we
all turned and made at the boat.
The bay swarmed with sharks, especially (it was
said) in the central part, into which flowed the
river and four or five creeks, the mouth of which
were the favourite haunts of fish.
The boat was a wretched old tub—a mere box,
built and tarred and pitched by the lads them'
selves, abominally leaky, and only susceptible of
any speed by reason*of the enormous stretch of
canvas she carried.
It was soon apparent that she would barely sup.
port us clinging on to her, let alone climbing upon
her, when at least our legs must hang in the water
as a bait to the sea-tigers—a most unnerving
reflection to me.
Horsford, the two lads, and I caught hold of
her bottom (keel she had none), and Syd. cried
out: —
“ Where’s Soule ?”
We all had a horrified, frightened look. Soule
couldn’t swim. He was the only one who couldn’t,
and there wasn’t a sign of him anywhere.
“Perhaps he’s under the boat,” said Tom.
“ Let’s try and turn her over.”
This I saw was only a waste of time.
The wind whistled and the feet of the squall
kept on stamping in the water, flattening it out
or churning it up, but the worst of it was past,
and I resolved to dive under the boat at once.
I shouted to Tom and Horsford, who were on
either side of me, and making no reply to Tom’s
exhortation to be careful, pushed off thrust
down my head, and with outstretched pointed
arms went under.
I came up on the other side a few yards off,
completely exhausted, and swam to the boat and
caught on next to Syd.
I shook my head.
“He’s not —there,” I shouted, breathing heavily,
“ I’m almost sure,”
We all had that soared look again, except Hors-
ford, who seemed taken up with something else,
or I do him an injustice.
Ten minutes or so later I dived again and Tom
followed me.
We both felt about under the boat, but could
find nothing.
The squall had now passed entirely, and there
was only a fresh wind blowing and a low, choppy
sea.
I was angry with Horsford and said to him
across the boat: —
“ Won’t you try, too?”
We looked at one another with all our old anta-
gonism and hatred.
“ No,” he said, sulkily; “ he’s drowned.”
And then we others began to realise the danger
and the misery of our situation.
11.
I must tell you something about Horsford and
myself.
Not much. For I don’t care to, as you will
understand when I begin by saying that we both
loved the same girl.
That is—Ethel, the lads’ sister. She married
Horsford.
This was two years ago, and it was her father
who managed it.
I don’t say Ethel loved me. I haven’t any right
to say so; but I believe that, if we had been left
alone and I had had fair play, things would have
ended differently.
Mr. Marquand is a business man, a station-agent
—one of the oldest-established firms in Brisbane,
and Horsford is his junior partner.
Hereford is a young Victorian and has plenty o
money, and (for his own reasons which were no
doubt as they were, in his own opinion, excellent)
he bought into the linn at the most critical moment
of its existence.
That was towards the close of the “ big drought.”
Bix months more of it, everybody said, would
have broken a good many stronger firms than
Marquand and Co., and Horsford (I am pretty
sure) saved it from bankruptcy.
My people have known the Marquands ever
since I can remember, and though we have grown
steadily poorer and they steadily richer, my
brother and I never knew, at any rate on the part
of Mrs. Marquand, Ethel and the lads, what it
was not to be welcome at their home.
Mr. Marquand (I can’t tell you why) was never
cordial to me ; but then he was never cordial to
anyone—except Horsford.
It is a long story, but it can easily be made a
short one.
Horsford became powerful at the Marquand’s,
and he soon made up his mind to “jump my
claim.”
I hate fashionable Victorians. They are such
snobs. They are very little better than English
people.
Horsford’s airs and graces sickened me, and the
lads, too; but something had come over Ethel.
I can’t explain it. X didn’t know, X only sus-
pected.
Anyhow, the result of his antagonism to me
could not long be doubtful. One of us had to go,
and he was the stronger.
He was always there, and always aggressive
against me, and, of course, I retaliated.
Then the family bought a house and a piece of
ground at Sandgate, and went down to live at the
Brisbane seaside resort (Sandgate was still going
ahead then before the other lines were opened up),
and the next thing was the announcement of his
engagement with Ethel.
That finished me.
I went off to Sydney full of bitterness and bad
temper, and I wrote a letter from there to her
which I shall regret until my dying day,
Horsford and she were married, and they went
to Melbourne (of course) for their honeymoon.
When I came up to Brisbane last autumn to
spend my holidays with my people, I had made up
my mind that I would see neither the Marquands
nor the Horsfords.
The Horsfords were living at Sandgate, too,
but right out of the town.
I went to Sandgate with my brother on a short
visit to the Bridges—he is engaged to Gerty
Bridge.
There I met the lads Tom and Syd., and I could
not break right away from them. They would
not let me. One thing led to another. They had
made a sailing-boat for themselves—the coffin
they called it, in jest, as it was long and black —
and they were always out in it with their friend
Soule, and they wanted me to come.
At last I agreed for that Saturday afternoon,
and they were to bring her along to the pier,
where I would join them.
When they arrived I saw that Horsford held the
tiller.
It appeared that Mrs. Marquand was anxious
about them, the weather being so uncertain, and
he insisted upon accompanying them.
That was the explanation he deigned to give me
in his sulky, supercilious way, when I got in, and
I have no doubt he knew as well as I did how
much I believed it.
111.
It was about an hour off sunset.
The day was exceptionally mild—hot even—
more like December or January than April.
So far I had not felt the coldness of the water
in the least. It was even pleasant.
But I knew that when the sun sank behind the
city and the dusty Bunya hills, and the western
night-breeze began to blow off the land, it might
become exceedingly cold.
The sea was quite calm and seemed to grow
calmer and calmer.
Unfortunately, we were drifting steadily right
out of the track of the steamers which traverse
the central and southern part of the bay. We
were going more and more north.
Not a sail —a funnel—was to be seen. Only far
off, beyond the islands to the south-east, a trail of
smoke floated, blurred and diffused—evidently
some steamer down at the bar passing out and
away.
t spoke to Tom.
“ Shall we stick here,” I said, “or swim for it ?
The chances are about equal.”
“ Syd. couldn’t manage it, ’he answered, “and
there’s the sharks.” I recognised the meaning of
the look on his face.
As we passed by St. Helena, the penal settle-
ment, he remarked to me that it was said, of all
the convicts who had escaped and tried to swim
from there to the land only one had succeeded,
and it was supposed that sharks were responsible
for the fact. On the other hand I thought I would
sooner chance them than remain in the water all
night and perhaps half the next day. Besides,
what could hinder them from grabbing us where
we were—for the coffin barely supported our
weight scarcely an inch of her being above water ?
The death of Soule and the realisation of our
danger had unnerved us. We remained quite still
and silent for several minutes. Then, once more
I proposed to swim for it and asked Syd. if he
thought he could do it? We could, I said, float
and rest wherever he wanted. But the lads were
afraid; they preferred to stick to the boat and
hoped to be sighted and picked up by someone.
The moon was already high in the heavens, and,
though in her last quarter, would, shining from
that cloudless sky and upon the waveless sea, give
us a fair chance.
This was Tom’s idea, but it struck me as ex-
tremely probable that after sunset we whould have
a winter haze rising up all over the land and hang-
ing over the water. For there had been a good
deal of rain during the last fortnight.
We spoke very little.
Horsford indeed never once broke silence but
clung on opposite me with his head and body
turned away, frowning with a sullen expression, as
I saw now and again as the hulk slewed round a
little.
We were still drifting north, being evidently in
the middle of a current and impelled by the
slight breeze from the south.
Then the sun dropped in a golden mist behind
the hills. The light waned, then grew clearer
again in the after-glow, and finally died away.
Syd.’s teeth were chattering loudly, and his face
and hands were like those of a corpse. At last he
said : “I can't hold on much longer, Tom.”
I let go and swam round to him and, with
Tom’s help, lifted him up higher into the hulk.
The poor little lad was quite exhausted, and I
felt sure that the coffin was unable to support the
whole of his weight. And so it turned out.
What was to be done ?
Tom and I got on either side of him and sup-
ported him, but soon he altogether let go with his
hands, and the strain of his weight upon us was
dreadful.
Hours passed, or so it seemed. The moon shone
out down in the west, and, as I had expected the
sky became thick with haze. I felt quite numbed
and paralysed.
Syd. seemed unconscious, lying heavily back
upon me.
The stars were coming out quickly all over the
sky, while the haze grew denser and spread more
and more across the water. At last you could
scarcely see the land at all, and round us it was
almost dark.
Suddenly Tom whispered hoarsely : “I must let
go, Bob.” And before I could answer a word we
all three fell backwards into the water and went
under.
I clutched at Syd. with my left hand, but he
got loose, being held, I supposed, by Tom.
I cannot say what happened for several minutes
then. It was all like a horrible slow nightmare-
dream in which I laboured heavily.
When I got back to the punt I saw Tom a little
way off, swimming wearily like a dog.
I called to him ; he saw me, turned a little, and
approached. His breath came and went in rat-
tling spasms. He said something, but my own
noisy breath and the whirling in my ears prevented
me from hearing what it was.
He repeated it. “ Where’s Syd. ?” he said.
All I could reply was to groan out : “ My God !”
I wish I had died first.
Without a word, he lifted up his hands over bis
head and went straight down.
And I could do nothing. That was the terrible
part of it. I was numbed and paralysed. I closed
my eyes and thought of following him.
IV.
I do not know what did it or how long after this
it was, but the time came when I all at once
p eemed to awake with a start.
I found Horsford staring at me across the hulk.
I could see his face dusky-grey with a faint light
in it.
I could sea his eyes, and recognised how hate
fully they were staring at me.
The sea all round was lit up with phosphor-
escence. Little points of it, like handfuls of millet-
sead, ware strewn here and there, and the outline
of the boat, where it gently rose and fell and
struck the water, was like bright silver.
The moon was approaching the edge of a
great haze and shone clearly, surrounded by the
larger stars in a faint irregular white spheroid,
while outside this the other stars in masses scin-
tillated brilliantly, particularly in the extreme
north, south, and east. We were quite motionless,
or, if anything, were drifting slowly westward
towards the land, despite the contrary evening
breeze.
The whole thing grew upon me. It had struck
him from the first. The tide was on the turn.
We had fallen away from the northerly back
current, and were now about to be carried by the
flow right into the land. He had foreseen this,
and realised that being saved was merely a
question of endurance. He had, therefore,
doggedly refused to waste one jot or tittle of his
strength in an effort to save the rest, or any of us.
Do not think me mad when I say that, as we
two clung there and stared at one another, I could
feel his heart and brain beating against mine.
An hour dragged on. Two hours. Three, for
all I can tell. It appeared more like thirty. We
were moving steadily westwards —backward and
forward—staring at one another, rigid with cold
and hatred.
He was asking himself when I should let go and
sink ? And I was making up my mind that if I
felt myself failing, I would swim round and seize
him and drag him down with me.
I thought of abominable names for him—coward,
hound, skunk and villain were the mildest—
abusing myself as a besotted fool for not having
understood it all before.
And still we clung there, staring at one another.
Suddenly I began to laugh. It was not only for
life and the sake of life that we were fighting this
tremendous duel. It was for love, and the sake
of love. On that far shore to which we were
steadily but surely drifting, there stood the form
of the woman we both loved. She was for the
survivor, the conqueror.
What made him sick with rage was the idea
that if he perished and I lived to marry her I
should have all his money as well.
That was what made me laugh.
I would not have touched a penny of his if I
were starving; but it was glorious to let him
torture himself with the idea that I would get it
all. That hurt him even more than the idea of
my marrying Ethel.
Oh, how I read and understood their married
life now! How I realised the abject failure of
this cowardly brute to break the spirit of the girl
whom he had won by such infamous means !
Yes, she had loved me all along. I saw it now.
I saw her sacrifice herself to save her father from
bankruptcy and ruin, and, perhaps, worse (dark
and sinister rumours which I had heard and
rejected came back to me). Oh, my brave mv
beautiful love !
The moon had fallen right into the bank of the
haze which was rising imperceptibly. And now
she was near the hills behind the far-off city, shin-
ing feebly, a dusky-gold and rayless crescent. The
stars above us were clear and innumerable right
down to the edge of the haze, where they shone
few and faint and presently disappeared. The
hulk was turning upon itself, drifting along end-
ways, instead of sideways.
Both of us were desperately weary. I fell at
last, despite all I could do to prevent it, into a
sort of sleep or stupor.
I breathed heavily and regularly to a tune which
I have forgotten, but which repeated itself again
and again and again—perpetually, endlessly.
Once or twice I wondered to myself if both of us
would let go soon and fall asleep together in the
water ?
I forgot all about him. I hated him no more. I
even liked and pitied him. I shut my eyes and let
my cheek rest on the wood of the hulk, and then
for some time I forgot. • Perhaps I really did fall
asleep, I cannot say.
I was awakened by the rocking of the boat. I
looked and saw that he was trying to climb up
into it.
He was quite weak and worn-out; as weak and
worn out as I was.
The moon had set. There was no light but that
of the stars and of the phosphorescence in the water,
but several of the stars were very brilliant and had
long wakes. I could see all the outline of his body
and limbs in silver as he moved trying to climb up.
I merely watched him. I could do nothing, or
at any rate I did not care to try.
At last he got up and the hulk sank lower.
He was at the bow and balanced himself, but
did it clumsily for he had not the strength to get
over it; he sprawled right across in pulling him-
self up. His hand touched my arm and he
stopped. This was the last round of our death
duel, and we knew it; but were both utterly ex-
hausted.
If he got there and kept there, what then ? I
tried to think what I should do.
Before I could make up my mind (I was still
half-asleep, or seemed so) he lifted himself up and
struck me with his right hand half clenched.
I moved aside; the hulk rocked; he swayed,
and toppled right over into the water.
I merely watched him —fascinated.
He sank slowly, with his arms stretched out and
up. His face was towards me, his head thrown
back. He was completely outlined in the phos-
phorescence. I could see every outspread finger—
his mouth—his eyes—his hair. Three streams of
small silver bubbles ran forth from his mouth and
nostrils as he went steadily down—down-down—
oh, my God ! down into the darkness of the pit.
It was beautiful and terrible beyond expression.
Shall I always see the arms and outspread
fingers stretched up to me—these streaming bub
bles from the mouth and nostrils of that head
thrown back ?
I shut my eyes, clinging to the hulk, and tried
not to see them, but to sleep and forget.
But when someone seized me (it was Stephens,
the Pine Creek fisherman) and dragged me into
his boat (which I remember vaguely, and also the
grey dawn-light instead of the stars and haze), I
still saw them a,nd besought him to jump in and
dive and pull him out.
You know the rest.
FRASCIS ADAMS.

WOMAN’S EIGHTS— BY A MALICIOUS MAN.


A CRIME
IN
INK
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When your generous offer of £5 weekly
for a series of short stories came, sir, Florence
(my wife) and I decided, after some discussion, to
accept it. “You shall do the men,” she said,
“and I will do the women ; and as for the local
colouring that he talks about, we can manu-
facture that.” When I suggested that I might
possibly help her with the women, knowing some-
thing of the sex, she threw me a smile tinged with
satire. Anyhow, your offer was accepted, and
that afternoon we ordered a new drawing-room
carpet and a piano on the strength of it.
You will remember how well things went for
the first three weeks. “The Lucky Digger,”
“The Reformed Gambler,” and “The Repent-
ant Prodigal ” have, as short stories, I venture to
say, seldom been equalled in English literature.
They were the outcome of a system. We
Florence and I, slept with note-books beneath our
pillows, and one or other of us would awaken
frequently during the night with a “Sh!!—l
have an idea.” We rode by night on the front
seats of trams and fed our brains on fish, hence the
excellence of those ideas.
* * «
Well, sir, the fourth week came, and it
was my turn to find the plot. My conception was
of a widower of 42 and his son of 21 living to-
gether on terms of perfect equality as men about
town, sharing alike sorrows and joys, loves and
and dinners, incomes and sodas. I introduced, of
course, a girl, a clean, fresh, primrose-scented
thing from the country. She sold flowers from a
shop in Burlington Arcade and my heroes fell
simultaneously in love with
her; but their loves were as
pure and chaste as the
flow'ers she sold. The sub-
ject was never mentioned
between them until one day
they met at the Criterion
corner. Both had been to
see her. “Look here,” the
old fellow said, as he took
the youngster’s arm, with a
kindly pressure, “it is ab-
surd our fooling about Alice
any longer. I hate compet-
ing with you. Let us make
a dead-beat of it and edu-
cate her between us.”
And so £2OOO was put
aside and the girl sent to a
private school at Highgate.
All the week she worked at
her lessons, and on Sundays
came down to their chambers
in Gray’s Inn, when the two
men, Naaman-like, plunged
into the torrent of her clean
love for them. Then, in
fulfilment of the drama’s
necessity, bankruptcy over-
took the men, and the girl
came into money, and the
completion of the story
demanded that she should
marry one of them.
I looked across to Florence
and asked “ Which?”
She, somehow, had fallen
desperately in love with the
old fellow, and decided, in
the spasmodic way most
women have, that the girl
should be his.
To me this was unnatural
and a sin against art and
I tried in a gentle way to
lead her to my way of think-
ing. She was first obstinate,
then angry and at last tempestuous, and, rising
from her chair, marched to the door, and, swing-
ing round, said with the slow, calm eloquence of
autocracy : “As the girl is of my making, I sup-
pose I can do as -I like with her, and I have
finally decided that the elder man shall marry
her.” With this she left me, and for hours I
heard her pacing her bedroom muttering I know
not what.
As for me, I paced the study and wrestled with
the destiny of these people. Suddenly a golden
means suggested itself. The girl should marry
neither, but die to slow music, leaving her vast
wealth to be equally divided between them.
And the two men, the old and the young,
should mingle their tears over her grave and be
bound together by the everlasting bond of a
common sorrow.
Impelled by this inspiration, Alice died rapidly
I killed her at twelve that night, and, placing
t he MS. in my desk, I went to bed, but not to
leep. The curse of Cain hung about me as a
garment, and every approach to unconsciousness
was rendered hideous and dreadful by the sad,
reproachful face of the dead Alice peering
through the darkness with an et iu Brute expres-
sion.
Towards morning tiredness won for me
an hour of sleep, and I awoke at half-past five
with a start to find myself alone. Florence had
gone. I rushed to the study and found her stand-
ing over the desk on which lay the story. She
was a personification of Millais’ “Yes or No,”
soaked in sadness. Turning to me as I entered,
she said in clear, deep cut, copper-plate, simply
this, “So you have killed her ! May God foi’give
you !” and left the room.
Although I had felt like a whipped hound
before, I had never realised until that moment the
actual depth of degradation to which I had sunk-
I had, in cold blood and at midnight, murdered
this young, innocent thing of our own making.
A restlessness like that of Eugene Aram took
possession of me. I dressed hurriedly, and tried
in the open air to find some forgetfulness. In
vain. At half-past eight I returned to find
Florence gone. She had left a note on the dining-
room table, saying that she “ had decided to stay
for a while at the Rogers’, not feeling quite safe in
the house after what had happened .”
This was on the Tuesday. That day, Wednes-
day and Thursday formed a trinity of black-letti r
days never to be forgotten. I wrote to her
repeatedly, imploring her to return. I said —
“Only come back to me and the girl shall marry
whomever you may choose.” I said—“ Come,
only come, Florence, and see me wash away this
blood-stain in the salt tears of repentance, and I
will do anything and everything by way of repai’a-
tion.” To these letters I received no reply.
* * *
One day I met in the street a son of my
wife’s host, who “cut me dead.” This was too
much. That I had been guilty of a gross
crime against Florence there was no disputing.
That I had murdered the child of her brain,
loved by her well and wisely, was a truth burnt
into my memory by each succeeding hour of con-
sciousness. But what had this interfering fool to
do with it! The girl was our own, a denizen of a
world where Florence and I reigned supreme,
giving unto this one wealth, to another poverty,
throwing laughter here, tears there, and weeding
out the impossible with the hoe of Death, and his
interference was simply impious. I turned and
followed him, demanding some explanation.
“ Turning to me as I entered the said, ‘So you have killed her! May God forgive you !’”
“What right have you,” Tasked, “to treat me
in this way ? ”
“Aright,” he Replied, “in common with the
rest of my sex to protest against the illtreatment
of a defenceless woman.”
“ Which of them ? ” I hissed, presuming that he
knew all.
He curled his lip and said, “You need not fear
that your confidence has been broken. My sisters
have gleaned their information from the night-
ravings of a woman’s brain, tortured with the
memory of some hideous injustice done to one
named * Alice.’ Now do you understand ? ”
I left him and went back to my loveless home,
threw myself upon the couch and stared at the
ceiling, and saw there a possible door of escape
gradually open itself. The darkness faded, a
delicious sense of coming pleasure pervaded the
room through. I saw in the resurrection of Alice a
possible renewal of the old order. That night
Alice rose again, and the younger of the two
Bohemians died, and died ,as heroes should,
slowly, poetically, and of consumption. Through-
out his illness the two of them took him to little
dinners at Ivetner’s, matinees, and for drives in
the Park. Then he sank away in the old
chambers, on cither side of him the others stood,
and as his breath came and went fitfully, and that
light, the newer light of another world, kindled
in the bottom of his eyes, he joined their hands,
and, turning to the old fellow, said, “We have
ate, drank, loved, laughed, lived, and liked life
well; but it is all over now. Take her, take care
of her for both oursakes”—and then passed away.
# * *
I finished the story, and taking the last
train to town, found you, if you will remember,
sir, in your den. You know how I pleaded for
its immediate publication, how I fell upon my
knees and told you that the happiness of a whole
family was at stake. Your good heart gained the
upper hand of you, and on the Thursday the story
of “Tithonus Junior” went forth to the world.
I need scarcely say the goddess, Sleep, forsook me
that night. It was half-past five before I could
get a copy of your paper, the paper that was
to bring peace and a wife back to me. I took
it to the Gardens and told the trees and
flowers of it, and the flowers dried their eyes.
Above me was a new heaven, and beneath abetter
earth, and all sang to me with one accord—
“ Alice lives! Alice lives!” and the trees whis-
pered the same glad tidings.
At half-past ten I was pacing the drawing-room
in happy expectation of something—what I hardly
knew—when the bell rang and the girl handed
me a telegram worded as follows : “ Come and see
your wife at once.” The signature was that of a
local medical man. Another hour found me at
the Rogers’ gate, and there pacing the lawn was
the doctor himself. Noticing his anxious face I
rushed to him with “Has anything serious
happened ? ” “ No,” he replied, with a tremulous
sigh of relief. “The worst is over, the crisis past,
but stay here, I will announce you.”
When he returned he told me the terrible his-
tory of Florence’s illness; how she had come
down to a condition of utter prostration, and
drifted slowly into brain-fever, of her ravings
about some injustice done, some murder perpe-
trated.
In the dining-room I heard of my poor wife’s
recovery. The acute attack had lasted, it
appeared, until half-past eight that morning,
when she fell into a quiet sleep. Miss Rogers
and the nurse were standing at the foot of the bed,
and talking in an undertone of some story in that
morning’s paper, when suddenly the patient
awoke with a start, and shouted in a half-maniacal
way, “ Bring it to me, read it to me! ” The nurse
thought it wise to accede to her request. The
story was read, and with this marvellous result
that when the wedding of some stupid couple was
reached the patient burst into tears, and the crisis
was passed.
“Eu fc follow me, you shall see her yourself.”
said Miss Rogers.
* * #
The room into which they led me was
daikened, and it was some seconds before my eyes
were sufficiently accustomed to the light to realise
my surroundings. Then I saw the trained nurse
in uniform moving with that alarming stealthy
tread of her tribe. On the table a basket of white
flowers, an ice-bag, some champagne and medicine
and on the bed Florence herself, one cold, ivory-
white shoulder peeped out, and about it her
goiden hair lay thick and radiant like sunbeams
that blushed when caught in the act of kissing
some chaste mountain top while men slept. The
great eyes of her moved in the quiet face as
restive prisoners who peep between prison-bars.
With a languid wave of her hand she dismissed
the nurse and Miss Rogers, and then beckoned me
to come round to her. I fell upon my knees at
her pillow s side, and for a few moments we were
snent as she ran her fingers through my curls
carrying love and forgiveness. Then she drew my
head nearer and, with what strength she had,
whispered : lam so-so-happy-and lam sure
Alice is—l thank you for her sake; ” then, after a
pause, and in a suppliant way : “Don’t vou think
dearest, it would be safer if for the future we con-
fined ourselves strictly to Comedy ? ”

WALTER L'ESTKANGE.


Another Esau!
A Tale of a Trip Home.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A dreary open plain in Northern Victoria,
pustuled over with yellow mounds of earth, where
men have dug for alluvial gold—and put their
hopes “to bed with a shovel ” —around them the
black crows as mutes still lingering, and no
vegetation but here and there a gum-tree. No
habitation but our hut, no colour but the dark
sage-green body-pigment that Nature rubs into
new canvas before she paints her flowers, and no
life but our two selves, and one of us dying ; such
were the surroundings.
The man dying was my mate, Jim. Jim was a
name of my selecting, and fitted him, for, like
another Jim of whom we all have read, he was
“long-legged and ornery.” His father who, at the
date of registration, had not my advantage of
seeing him as an adult, had christened him
Marriott Barry Gilmore.
Jim and I had prospected together for ten years,
and barely earned “ tucker,” and now, just as we
had made our first real find in the ranges beyond,
and were waiting for the assay, Jim had caught
the fever and was babbling of his people at
home. At his request I had been reading to
him some of his mother’s letters —religious letters
into which God’s universal love for men had
been focussed by a mother’s heart. I had finished
the last of them ; young, wholesome Morning,
hating Death, was lingering behind the hills ; the
winds, waiting with their chariot for the soul of
the man, ran round and round the house, shook
the windows and doors and flapped the roof in
their impatience, when Jim gathered together
what of life he had, turned round and, giving me
his hand, said, “ If the reef does carry a lot, don't
forget them —good-bye.” The winds quieted
down, and the day came to find me alone.
# » #
The reef, the since celebrated Lone Hand,
did carry a lot. It was two feet thick six feet
down, and at the water-level was half as wide
again, and crushed 4oz. to the ton. Six montl s
from Jim’s death I sold out for £50,000,
and stood, an hour after I had deposited the
money in the bank, at the corner of Collins-street,
Melbourne, the loneliest and most miserable man
on the face of God’s earth. During the long
years that I had been hunting for gold, I had
been dropping friends ; all but Jim had left me,
and now, just as luck came, he went. I had lost
more than my friends —I had lost my social
instincts, my capacity to love and be loved ; I
had no vices, and yet I was a social outcast and a
millionaire, I wandered into Scott’s and “ordered
something as I sat in the vestibule, thinking of
Jim, the idea seized me that, next to the old
chap’s self, I would like to see his people, who,
although I had never met them, were, from years
of converse, well-known to me—Jim’s solid father,
his good mother, and Florence, the red-haired
baby, had come to be spoken of as though my
own relations. So I decided to go home at once,
and look them up.
The feeling of loneliness that, like a malignant
entity, lay somewhere near my heart, grew
heavier and heavier as I neared England. On
board ship the ladies, whose hands I would have
given anything to touch, avoided me—empty-
headed, well-dressed, little globe-trotters made
all the running, and I loathed them. In London
it was worse. Even the cabmen thought an
invitation to ride would be wasted enterprise.
The people in Regent-street stared, and the waiters
made remarks to each other; so, in two days I
started for Sussex to look my old mother up.
Eighteen years I had been away, and yet the old-
fashioned village nestled in the hollow of the hills
was as though I had left it but yesterday; the
cows were feeding as then; the windmill on the
hill might never have stopped; farm-labourers
trudged home from work with trousers tied around
below the knee ; the little trout-stream, that I had
dreamt of so often in the scorching days and
nights of Australia, sang the same old song. I
stopped on the bridge a moment and asked a
labourer who passed me if it (the stream) held any
trout. “It did, but not of late years.” I led him
on a bit, and he told me that “ he minded the
time that young Charley Dawson (that’s me) used
to catch a couple of dozen afore breakfast. ”
“ Where is young Dawson now ?” I asked,
“ Oh, he went to ’Stralia and left his old mother,
who lives in the cottage there” (pointing to a
two-roomed hut near). “ She’s on the parish, so
he warn’t o’ much account.”
“Butwhat about the money she had?”
“She took a little farm called Ockham, and lost
it all.”
Then I walked on to the cottage and found
mother sleeping at the fireside with her mouth
open.
After a time I succeeded in awakening her and
made her understand that I was her son Charley.
She called me “ Sir,” and said she was glad I had
eome back, as the damp had taken to strike up
from the kitchen floor, and I might do something.
“You was always handy with your hands.” She
asked me how I left all my friends, and if they
had much “ rheumatiz” out there.
I made all arrangements for mother’s comfort,
and the following afternoon found me at Sheer-
in-Surrey, and near the little farm where Jim’s
people lived. As I neared the house I could see
two women whom I rightly conceived to be
Florence and her mother watering flowers upon
the lawn. The old lady was in her second child-
hood, and Florence (I knew her by her red hair)
helped with exceeding tenderness to carry her
watering-can from place to place. I reached the
wall without being observed and sat down under
it for ten minutes—another Peri seeking Paradise.
The wall was but four feet in height, but it
separated God's best gift, the quiet content of
domestic love, from the earth’s worst curse, the
inaudible groanings of a social derelict.
Suddenly the voices stopped, and I rose. The
mother had gone in, and the girl was in the door-
way—
Half light, half shade,
She stood, a sight to make an old man young.
Seeing me she walked across to where I was stand-
ing and asked me if I wanted to see her father.
Driven by some sudden, inexplicable impulse, I
looked her straight in the face and said, “ Yes, I
am his son, your brother Marriott.”
Florence at first stepped back, as though to run
in doors and announce me, then her good heart
drove her to the wall, and leaning over she threw
her arms about my neck and drew me into Jim’s
heritage, the Heaven of her love for him.
“ You must not come in yet,” she said, when
the first rush of her welcome was over, “ father is
very ill, and the shock might be too much for him.
Stay hero, I will see mother.”
In five minutes the old lady, who had grown
grey praying for us, was crossing the lawn
to meet me, and then my face was drawn
down by her long, thin fingers to hers, and held
there till the trembling was past; then, with a
deep breath, she released me. “Marriott, my
prayers are answered —your father will see you
before lie dies and give you his blessing.”
Hand in hand we walked across the lawn and
into the dining-room. As we entered she whis-
pered, “ Your father is paralysed, but I think he
will know you.” In the square chimney-corner,
with Florence kneeling on the farther side of him,
the old man sat staring at the smouldering logs
and trying to understand something of the strange
news the girl had told him—news of his son’s
return.
“Here ho is, father, so strong and well. He
will help you with the farm, and it will begin to
pay again, and you will get better, and we shall
all be so happy,” and she stroked the lifeless
hand nearest her. At the old woman’s bidding, I
knelt, too, and she, bending her mouth to his ear,
HERE’S PATHOS FOR YOU.
OLD DRUNK; “I’ve not much longer to
live, your Worchip; God will not
spare me many hours.”
WORTHY MAGISTRATE (choking with emotion ):
“Well, I’ll give you ten days.”
said—“ John, here’s Marriott come back—wont
you bless him ?”
He turned his head round towards me, and
peered inquiringly as through a fog; then a ripple
of consciousness ran through his years, and the
hand nearest me, that had some life left, moved
as though to find me in his darkness; the old lady
gently bent my head and placed his hand on it.
Then he muttered something and fell back.
« * *
Strange as it may appear, in the delicious
joy of a common sorrow and the excitement of
the few days that followed old Gilmore’s death,
which took place the next day, the money
question was either forgotten or thought
of only when I was alone. Sometimes the
three of us would sit together in the chimney
corner, and while one of my hands was being
fondled by the old lady, the fingers of the other
would lose themselves in Florence’s bounteous
hair; at others, my new sister and I would
wander about the garden, through the dairy, or
over the farm, intensely, if silently, happy. The
fact was, Peri had found a paradise, whose streets
were paved with something better than gold.
Old Gilmore died on the Tuesday, and on the
following Saturday he was borne on the shoulders
of his labourers to his first freehold.
The funeral over, I had time to look round and
re-ebnsider my position.
To me the most shocking feature of it was that
my love for Florence had outgrown the limits of
brotherhood, and my own deception had made all
other dreams impossible, I could not go back
now and say “ Florence, I am not your brother; I
have crept into your heart and taken your father’s
blessing under false pretences,” and so write
myself down a liar.
Thoughts of this order rushed in upon me as I
wandered back from the churchyard. The will
was, I remembered, to be read at three o’clock ;
and, from hints that had been dropped, I fancied
the old man’s monetary affairs were in anything
but a satisfactory condition ; but this fact should
be rather helpful to me than otherwise under the
circumstances. With the material at my disposal it
should be possible to build a golden bridge across
that terrible abyss that separates the kingdom of
brotherhood from that other land the lover
dreams of.
As I neared the door ( saw Florence awaiting
me. “ Can you,’’she said, “ postpone your dinner
for half-an-hour, and come with me to the summer-
house? ”
“ I can, Florence; but why do you look so white
and anxious ? ”
“You will not wonder when you hear our
father’s will.”
“Never mind the will, Florence, I will put
that all right.”
“You, Marriott! You, poor boy! Ah, you
little know.”
We were in the summer-house now, and Florence
standing in front of me ; suddenly she threw her
arms around my neck and burst into tears. Then,
when her sobbing was over, she sat beside me and
said, “You don’t know what it is for me to have
a brother again, and yet for your own sake I wish
you had not come. ’Tis not fair that you should
start a new life with a mother and sister on your
hands and debts to pay.” Then she leaned on my
shoulder again and cried bitterly ; “ Poor father,
it killed him, and it will kill mother, too, when
she hears the worst.”
Slowly the fact dawned on me that this next
hour was to be my Waterloo; but the winning
of it demanded more generalship than I felt
possessed of.
“Florence, will you run indoors and let me
think this matter over ? ”
She kissed me and left for the house, but before
he had gone 20 y;irds I called her back, and put-
ting my hands upon her shoulders said : “At a
rough guess how much do you think it will take
to square up father’s affairs ? ”
Florence turned white and red alternately, and
then, taking one lapel of my coat in each of her.
hands, leant forward and, looking upon the
around, said : “ I don’t know—l am afraid to tell
you; but, I should think, two thousand pounds ”
I could not refrain from laughing two
thousand pounds, and I had twenty-five thou-
sand of hers at my disposal at that moment.
“ Marriott, you are either very hardened or
very mysterious ; to me this condition of things is
absolutely hopeless. ”
She was leaving ; but the temptation to ante-
date my prerogative by just a few hours was
irresistible; so, drawing her to my breast, I kissed
her teal’s away, called her “ my own ” (omitting
the “sister ”), and told her not to be afraid —that
something, I was sure, would turnup ; and then I
sent her away.
There were only ten minutes before the meeting
commenced, and no plans could be formed in that
time, so I ran in the back way up into my room to
brush my hair.
In the next room there were two loud-voiced
men, whose conversation was distinctly audible.
They, I gathered, were the executors, and one of
them spoke very angrily of some bill for six hun-
dred pounds, for which he had no security.
Putting my cheque-book in my pocket I followed
these men down stairs.
In the little room opposite to the dining-room, I
saw Florence talking to some stranger, whose
back was towards me. She heard my step, and,
turning round, rushed out; her face was aglow
with smiles, brilliant as the rising sun. “ Some-
thing has turned up!” she whispered, and was
gone again.
In the room some half-a-dozen men were sitting
around the table; the old lady was at the head of
it, and as I entered she motioned me to take the
seat on her right. Then Florence came, and with
her a man—a square-shouldered son of the sea, in
captain’s uniform. They took two seats on the
old lady’s left.
Florence’s eye was still sparkling with some new
joy. I saw that her hand was wandering beneath
the table, and, finding his, both faces brightened
simultaneously. In that moment the truth
dawned, and the light of my life went out.
The man at the bottom of the table opened the
proceedings by reading the will.
Old Gilmore had left the whole of his property
to his wife, for her lifetime, and at her death it
was to be equally divided between his son Mar-
riot, if still living, and his daughter Florence. He
directed that the family Bible, his watch and
chain and seals, should be sent to his son at once.
As the executor closed the will he coughed
significantly and, as wo all know, was about to
explain the altered condit : on of the estate, when
the captain rose and my chance was gone. He
told them that as the fiancee of Miss Gilmore it
was his privilege to settle up the estate of the
deceased, and if any gentleman present had any
claim upon the estate he would gladly settle it,
either now or to-morrow morning.
From that the meeting drifted into a wine-
drinking, speech-making affair, which did not
interest me, I was about to leave the room when
Florence rose and, coming up to the back of my
chair, put one hand on both of my shoulders, and,
leaning down, whispered, “You said something
would turn up; come over and let me introduce
you to it.” Then she kissed me, and the face of
the man darkened.
I rose as freely as my heavy heart would allow
me, and followed her round to where he was
sitting, knowing that it was to meet my doom.
He rose at my approach and drew back from us.
“ I want to introduce my brother Marriott to
you, Alfred.”
Ere Florence could finish the introduction, he
of the square shoulders moved towards the door,
and when nearing it, turned round and said, “ I
should like to have a few words with you alone,
sir.” Then he led the way to the summer house,
and I followed like a hound already whipped.
Once well out of the house, he turned sharply
round on me.
“ I think, sir, you are playing the part of a
d d scoundrel. Marriott Gilmore died in Aus-
tralia, seven months since.”
“ He did—and I am playing his hand.”
“ And who are you?”
“ I am Charles Dawson, his partner.”
“ That’s another lie —Charles Dawson came
home with fifty thousand pounds.”
“ I am Charles Dawson, and have a bank receipt
for that sum in my pocket-but may I explain?”
“Explain! Explain!! I think if you have
that sum, and were about to let this poor family
be sold up, it would require more than even your
ingenuity to explain it satisfactorily. No, not
another word. I will give you half-an-hour; if
you are not gone then I will horsewhip you, and
then send for the police.”
He was leaving me, when I stopped him :
“May I not give Miss Gilmore a cheque for
her dead brother’s half ?”
His whole attitude reeked with contempt.
“Miss Gilmore’s hands are clean, and will
remain so.”
Again I followed him. “May I not wish her
good—”
Before I could finish he had felled me like a
bullock. When I came to myself I was alone j
under a hedge, near the house—if I had crawled
there or been helped I know not, but I was there—
and crept through it like a criminal sneaking from
justice. It was a summer’s night, but a summer’s
storm had gathered over me. As I trudged to the
station there quivered ever and again bright
flashes of lightning, like the rod of vengeance
Adam saw driving him from Eden. The heavens
wept, and I too; but mine were “salt tears
strange to shed. ”
In a half-numbed state I got back to London
and thence to Melbourne, and now my only hope
is that they may read this and forgive me, s°
“Home papers please copy,” I have sen* 1
Florence’s money to her father’s banker.
MANNINGTON CAFFYN.
A Mountain Station.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I bought a run a while ago,
On country rough and ridgy,
Where wallaroos and wombats grow—
The Upper Murrumbidgee.
The grass is rather scant, it’s true,
But this a fair exchange is,
The sheep can see a lovely view
By climbing up the ranges.
And “She-oak Flat’s ” the station’s name
I’m not surprised at that, sirs :
The oaks were there before I came,
And I supplied the flat, sirs.
A man would wonder how it’s done,
The stock so soon decreases—
They sometimes tumble off the run
And break themselves to pieces.
I’ve tried to make expenses meet,
But wasted all my labours,
The sheep the dingoes didn’t eat
Were stolen by the neighbours.
They stole my pears—my native pears
Those thrice-convicted felons,
And ravished from me unawares
My crop of paddy-melons.
And sometimes under sunny skies,
Without an explanation,
The Murrumbidgee used to rise
And overflow the station.
But this was caused (as now I know)
When summer sunshine glowing
Had melted all Kiandra’s snow
And set the river going.
And in the news, perhaps, you read :
“ Stock passings. Puckawidgee,
Fat cattle : Seven hundred head
Swept down the Murrumbidgee;
Their destination’s quite obscure,
But, somehow, there’s a notion,
Unless the river falls, they’re sure
To reach the Southern Ocean. ”
So after that I’ll give it best;
No more with Fate I’ll battle.
I’ll let the river take the rest,
For those were all my cattle.
And with one comprehensive curse
I close my brief narration,
And advertise it in my verse—
For Sale ! A Mountain Station.
BANJO.


His Brother Mason’s Wife.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
it was Unquestionably Wrong of Griffiths to Covet
his neighbour’s wife —but what else was a weak,
well-fed mortal, with a Christian name other than
Joseph, to do under the circumstances ? Pretty
Mrs. Poynter had such wicked little ways with
her, ways of which her devoted husband, G. Potts
Poynter, Esq., saw nothing and suspected less.
At convenient times she would be curious to know
what book Griffiths was reading, or what photo-
graph he was admiring, and her plump figure, as
she looked demurely over that gentleman’s
shoulder, would give him many gentle palpita-
ting reminders that she was there. She had a
habit, too, of brushing against Griffiths acci-
dentally in passages, and no stairs could have
been wide enough to enable him to avoid contact
with Mrs. Poynter when they happened to meet
by the way. If he courteously kept close to the
wall, his hostess didn’t trouble to hug the ban-
nisters, and their elbows, at least, were sure to
clash. Once Mrs. Poynter declared, with a side-
glance and blush, that he had knocked her funny-
bone, but Griffiths merely smiled and passed
upstairs. To say that the lady made overtures to
her guest in these early days of our story might
be uncharitable, but to say that she sorely tempted
him to offer what Smollett and Pielding called
“gallantries,” were an assertion warranted by
the facts of the case. Yet the poor fellow tried
to remain good, although he couldn’t keep from
coveting in secret.
They were brother Masons, Ohatles Griffiths
and Potts Poynter. They belonged to the same
lodge, had sworn the same strange oaths in the
same hall, and been mystically grilled on the
same gridiron —which, as every Mason’s wife
understands, is the solemuest of all rites inci-
dental to admission into the fraternity. Now, as
a man and a brother, Griffiths was not only dis-
qualified from assailing the virtue of Mrs. Poynter,
but bound to protect her against the wiles of a
wicked world at large, and, if necessary, save her
from herself. Therefore, every time that volup-
tuous creature nudged him casually in the gloam-
ing, or sighed like a furnace at him in the pianis-
simo part of a Griffiths would bite his
lip and curse inwardly. A handsomely-framed
certificate hung over Poynter’s sideboard, and its
large, all-seeing eye (like a horse’s) seemed to be
always on Griffiths.
The Potts Poynters, four years married, were
not troubled with a family. There were no fairy
footfalls tinkling on their tufted floor, or chubby
baby fingers grabbing at dada’s tufted chin.
When Potts laid his weary head upon the pillow
at night he knew, alas ! that no sweet little cherub
would start crowing into his ear about 3 a.tn., and
sometimes he indulged in vain conjectures respect-
ing his possible feelings as a father in the event of
the missing link being “about to teeth.” The
P.P.s, however, bore up very well against having
no children, and, in point of fact, rather revelled
in the calamity. Mrs. Poynter preferred single
young people and musical evenings to dull, mar-
ried acquaintances and private baby-shows, and
shuddered at the thought of a household treasure
chipping in its soprano robusto from an adjacent
bedroom when she was singing duets with her
favourite tenor.
Griffiths was that privileged vocalist. The
oldest of the young people (say a year or two over
30) he was the most inveterate attendant at these
evenings. About three times a week, too, on an
average, he dined with the Poynters enfamille.
Potts was very much attached to his old chum
Charlie, and had a fatal weakness for bringing the
silvery tenor home with him from the city, to try
over something new with his wife. Potts himself
being nothing better than a cracked baritone
with a chronic cough, was apt to leave the tuneful
pair trilling their lays whilst he pottered round in
the garden or went and yarned with the dog.
Love, beautiful love, was the inevitable theme
of duets between Griffiths and his friend’s wife.
If they sang solos, accompanying one another
upon the piano, it was with paeans of amorous joy,
or musical sobs of such secret passion and subdued
longing, that they shook the chandelier. This
was as much owing to force of circumstances as
to their own design. All the new ballads seemed
to deal with brand new “engagements,” or
aching hearts and livers depressed by grief.
Weeks and months ran smoothly along on the
lines laid down by the fatuous Potts. Friend
Griff, was continually being thrilled by the touch
of Mrs. Poynter’s white hand as they both
turned over a leaf of music at the same time, and
his soul chafed more and more against the statute
of limitations fixed by an unfeeling craft. The
temptation to slip his arm round the lady’s waist
and press her tight against his bachelor bosom
grew stronger with each new duet. He began to
loathe the very name of Masonry, except at
lodge dinners. The sight of a Past-Grandmaster,
or any sort of high chief cockalorem, was to him
as a red rag to a raging bull. He often felt in-
clined to fall bodily on a bloated autocrat of the
order in the middle of George-street, and ask him
whether he didn’t consider one particular law of
Masonry an outrage on human nature. As for
the beautiful example of Joseph, he had long
since decided that it was sickening.
One warm summer evening the pair were
languishing together at the piano, as usual.
Rain had just been falling after a six weeks’
drought, so the confiding but inhuman Potts,
armed with a bag of salt, was making the most
of such an opportunity to go snail-hunting in the
garden. By the pale light of a lantern
he was dropping grains of destruction upon
the slimy shell-backs and slugs, and emit-
ting fiendish chuckles as they petered
out of this life in yellow-greeny bubbles.
Occasionally he varied the monotony of the
slaughter by stamping on a large, fat snail in
order that he might hear its dying crackle and
gloat over the “ horrid swish ” of its splattered
entrails The master of the house was happy
enough.
Griffiths, playing his own accompaniment on
this occasion, had just concluded a really melting
appeal to “ Her whom I love with a love like fire,
with the passionate throb of a mad desire,” or
something of that sort—we have constructed the
poetry for this occasion. The thermometer stood
at 91 in the gas, and the bright colour in Mrs.
Poynter’s cheek came and went like a revolving
light. She was so impressionable; she had no
mother; Mrs. Grundy forgot her, and she fell to
kissing Griffiths fast and furiously. Bending over
that fascinating tenor as he sat, charged with
magnetism, upon the music-stool, she took his
face between her hands, pressed it backward a
little to the left, and attacked his mouth raven-
ously.
For several seconds Griffiths was carried away
by these unhallowed endearments, and returned
kiss for kiss. Then he shook himself free, stood
«P, and, facing his assailant, gasped: “Mrs.
Poynter — Maud — you mustn’t.” The words came
from his white, trembling lips like a despairing
groan. 18
“O, what have I done?” said the lady. Her
hands were before her face, and perhaps she took
one little peep at Griffiths through her fingers,
Poynter knew quite well what she had done,
and was all too ready to repeat the dose ; yet her
enquiry was per fectly feminine and natural. It is
the privilege of women to be able to delude them-
selves into astonishment at their own behaviour,
when astonishment seems called for.
Darling, you have shown me your heart, told
ffle at laBt %* th your own H (this was Griff.’s
o i rcumsp ect manner of putting the case) what I
have dared to suspect all along. You have made
me—at least, you would have made me the hap-
***' ? °“
She clutched the arm of her unlucky idol, and
hung her head a little. “I didn’t know—at least
I don’t quite understand—are yon angry with

serious, Masonic-brotherly voice he told her of the
awful barrier between them, of the everlasting
oath of allegiance which had placed every other
Maso"'. wife beyond the pale of hi. hopes, and
f ll " 1 upon her, with a gentle squeeze the while,
to observe that they could never, never be more

“ Oh, Charlie, how could you come to join such
a*wretched tiling, I always hated it! ” His ex-
planation hadn’t impressed the lady as much as he
expected. She looked almost sulky as he shook
her limp hand and rushed out to bid good-bye to
Potts, who was still busy amongst the snails.
That amiable husband, after escorting Griffiths to
the gate, returned to the drawing room and found
his wife sitting at the piano, apparently deep in
reflection. “ Naughty man, we began to think
you were lost,” she exclaimed.
The visits of Griffiths to his old friends grow
less frequent for a long time after the episode re-
corded above, whilst his absence from their
periodical gatherings was regretfully noted by the
brethren. And although the pressing invitations
of Poynter at length overcame his scruples as
regards the musical evenings, and tempted him
back to the house of danger as often as of yore,
nothing could revive his interest in the Craft,-
Friend Charlie excused himself from accompany-
ing Potts to Masonic dinners on the score of indi-
gestion. He didn’t dare to say aloud that Free-
masonry was “rot,” but he thought it all the
same. His disgust for the institution was almost
as great as that of Mrs. Poynter, who plainly told
her husband that no man who loved his wife
would belong to a secret society, and besought
him to give it up at once. It flattered him to see
how the little woman’s jaw dropped when he said
that withdrawal from the sacred bonds of Masonry
was impossible. “ Begad ! she does like to have
me always at home,” thought Potts.
All men are vain. . .
Autumn was far advanced in the sere,
yellow leaf, and Mr. Poynter was down wit
influenza, when Griffiths one morning received a
letter.

“ I wish you would get me a little poison from
your place. ” she said.
Griffiths, who earned his daily bread as acting-
manager of a wholesale drug business, was
not used to supplying his lady friends with that
class of goods.
“ Poison!” His thoughts naturally turned to
Rough on Rats.
“Yes, the dog howls at night and disturbs
Pottv (her pet name for Potts). Besides, he’s
getting very old, and I’m sure he would be better
killed ” —referring, of course, to the dog. Her
husband was in the prime of life.
“ Don’t you think you had better give the brute
away?” .
“ Who’d take him ? And then it would save
trouble to have him killed. I thought a few grams
of strychnine on a piece of meat —but you know
best. Perhaps there is something not so-so
violent as strychnine ; the same sort of thing, only
a little milder.” Mrs. Poynter shifted her position
several times whilst making the suggestion.
‘ Griffiths offered feeble objections to this mode
of dealing with the dog. He referred, in harsh,
jocular tones, to the danger of having deadly
poisons about a house, and suggested that the
butcher would give any domestic animal its happy
dispatch for a shilling. The lady remained ob-
durate, however.
“How foolish you are; as if there was any
danger.” She rose from her chair and put her
hand upon his shoulder. He took a rough hold of
her wrist, and they looked straight into one
another’s eyes, enquiringly.
“ Get it for rne, Charlie.”
“ If you must have it,” he answered.
His brother Mason’s wife drew a little nearer to
Charlie, as though expecting a more affectionate
reply, but he shook his head significantly, and
Maud knew he mustn't. But a moment after-
wards when he was leaving the house she put her
hand again upon his shoulder and whispered a
few words so softly that he had to bend his head
to catch their meaning.
Then she passed along into the bedroom a-tiptoe
and sat considering her liege lord as he lay asleep
and almost dead-looking. What did he want to
be a Mason for, she wondered.
The influenza, which affects so many different
people in so many different ways, soon began
to have a terribly depressing influence on
Mr. Potts Poynter, and at about six o’clock
on a cold, grey morning that respected mer-
chant stiffened himself out for ever. The
family doctor, a very gifted old gentleman,
ever on the alert for new marvels in Nature’s
ways, considered that the case of his friend was a
noble contribution to the cause of science, and a
further proof of the faculty s ignorance concerning
the vast possibilities of this new and mysterious
disease. He certified that Potts’ death was due
to acute tetanic influenza, and introduced his
symptoms into a lecture a few days after the sad
event. As one of the late gentleman’s executors
he found a melancholy pleasure in informing
enquirers that poor P. P. had left his distressed
widow amply provided for. “How cut up she
is !” he generally added. Ardent scientist that
he was, the doctor would willingly have seen the
late lamented cut up likewise, but his relict
wouldn’t hear of such a thing, it appeared,
“ The mention of a post-mortem sent her int*
hysterics—most devoted woman you ever met,’-
said the venerable geeser.
They gave Potts Poynter a fine Masonic
funeral, and a great concourse of gentlemen in silk
aprons and other uncanny trappings gathered
round his grave. Not one of his old friends was
missing from the ceremony save Charley Griffiths,
who had started North on a month's holiday
before poor Poynter’s bad symptoms set in.
General regret was expressed at 1 lie unavoidable
absence of the dead man’s dearest friend.
Wouldn’t Charlie be cut up when he heard of it,
they said. Everybody, indeed, was cut up.
When the chief mourners returned to the house,
for a little light refreshment and a parting sigh,
they remarked that the dog in the yard was
howling most pathetically. So it would seem
that Mrs. Poynter hadn’t poisoned the faithful
animal after all. She couldn’t find the heart to
do it, perhaps.
Mrs. Poynter took a trip to Maoriland almost
immediately after the funeral, and remained away
from Sydney a full six months. She bre her
sorrow with a gentle resignation and modest
fortitude which was considered to be beyond
criticism, and the monument over her late
husband’s grave was a chaste design in the best
red granite. Griffiths waited patiently for an
intimation of her return, which arrived at last.
They met where they had parted, in the same
room which had witnessed his last triumphant
struggle against temptation. She looked so nice
in her weeds.
“ There is no barrier between us now?” asked
Mrs. Poynter.
“None, none, my darling;” said the emanci-
pated Mason.
And that night, ere turning into his bachelor
couch to dream of an impending wedding, he
humbly thanked Heaven for having given him
strength to keep the solemn law of the craft.

Anguish of mind has driven many to
suicide, anguish of body never. This proves that
the health of the mind is of far more consequence
to our happiness than the health of the body,
although both are deserving of much more atten-
tion than either receives. In protracted cases of
disease it is the continual mental strain and worry
that enervates and finally undermines the whole
system and destroys the vital spark. How many
men apparently healthy to-day are lying in a cold
grave a week hence. They are negligent of their
health and think they can shake every attack off,
the effect of such foolishness becomes apparent to
every man sooner or later , and wise is he who never
neglects the symptoms of disease but takes pre-
cautions, as did a gentleman who writes:—“l
write to let you know that I am a patron of your
wonderful Clements Tonic; I have been a great
sufferer, and read of Mrs. Moeller's cure in the
newspapers, her case was nothing to mine, but,
thank God ! through her letter I am cured, and
after the doctors had pronounced sentence of death
on me. lam very grateful to Mrs. Moeller for her
letter. I have great pleasure in adding my
testimony to that of the many others cured of
serious diseases by the use of Clements Tonic; I
have suffered terribly from liver and kidney
disease. Two years ago my strength began rapidly
to decline, I had dull headache, completely lost
my appetite, and was almost blind—(failure of
sight is a symptom of Bright’s disease. —Ed). I
had tiger claw cramps in the calves of my legs,
and severe rheumatic pains, followed by diarrhoea,
strength continued to fail, accompanied by
extreme pallor of the face, puffed under the eyes,
and persistent swelling of legs, knees and ankles,
and my whole body was swollen to a wonderful
size ; sharp shooting pains pierced the heart and
frequently chills and fever would attack me.
The swelling was so bad that I was afraid
to even drink a glass of water. I consulted
a doctor, and he examined my water, and
he said it was a bad case of liver disease,
and also Bright’s disease of the kidneys; he
prescribed medicine and liniments with mustard
baths. I said, “ Doctor, if you will cure me, I
will PAY YOU WHAT YOU like.” He replied, 1
CAN HOLD OUT NO HOPE FOB you.” I said, “That
is very hard.” “I never like to take a mans
money,” said he, 11 without telling him the truth,
and I will come to you at any hour of the night
you may send for me. ” He thought I should soon
die. I gave his treatment a fair trial, but the
swelling of my body increased so much that I
had to" remain in bed; the pains increased and
extended all down the side, and my eyesight was
now almost completely gone. I consulted another
eminent medical man who, after sounding me and
testing my urine, said I had Bright’s disease.
I asked if he could cure me; he said he would do
his best, and if I would continue his medicine 1
should improve; I did so and it had but little
more effect than water. Two friends of mine
called my attention to Mrs. Moeller’s letter in the
paper who had been cured of the same disease by
Clements Tonic. I procured a supply, and
taking it strictly in accordance with the direc-
tions, the swellings began to go down. 1 kept up
the use of Clements Tonic, and now, thank God,
I am cured, my swellings have all subsided, my
eyesight is as good as ever* I took a good many
bottles, but that is nothing as it has cubed me,
and I cannot describe its value. .? ul ' s r , tru vv’
Thomas Irvine, Tatham, N.S.W.- F. M.
Clements, 212 A’Beckett-street, Melbourne.

“ Bob—l will write to—Bob !”
The sick man turned writhingly upon his hard
pallet There was only a blanket between his
body and the bed-frame, for the mattrass had been

his children for three days; and the withering
flesh was not so benumbed as yet by the pressed
fingers of approaching death that the roughness
and rigidity of the iron failed to inflict pain.
