Dear Robert,—The address is conventional and means
nothing, and, therefore, I use it. I don’t say “ Bob,” for
that would mean something. It would mean, would it
not, that there was a savour of the old days, our very
earliest days, when we cared tor one another, in my
nostrils ? And there is not, Robert—not the faintest!
As ever since elder boyhood, I hate you, and as you were
never the one to change, lam sure you hate me. If I,
who was always so variable, could be constant to that one
passion of hate, it is certain you hate me no less.
Then, still hating you, why should I write to you ? Not
for myself, you may be sure ! If you can answer this at
once, your reply will be delivered by Wednesday. This
is Monday-and by Thursday or Friday I shall be over
the Boundary. So, this is not for myself. It is for the
children ! And it is not for my share of the children, but
for her's. There are two of them, and I pray you to
recollect that they are her's as well asinine. A boy,
twelve, and a girl, nine-the boy with some rags upon
him, the girl with scarcely any; neither has had a full
meal for a month. I write for them, Bob.
There! the old boyhood’s name has slipped off the
nib. I did not know it, believe me, till it was done. I
got weak for the moment, thinking of the children there
must be some link between the present and the tender-
ness of that earliest time-and it went down. I will let
it stand. . ,
At the lowest ebb of the tide I write this—and there
will be no spring to the tide this time. I shall have ten
shillings—perhaps twelve-and-six —to-day from a certain
source. Eight of that sum will go for arrears of rent
and on the balance, whether it be two shillings or four-
and-six, the children and myself have to live till all is
over. When I’m beyond the Line— do you remember
how, on the home station, the old assigned cook used to
talk of dying as crossing the Boundary ? and we lads
didn’t understand him then ; we thought he mea "
going beyond the settled districts?— parenthetical, as
usual, every editor I have scribbled for says I am oo
parenthetical, and why shouldn’t I be?—l am only a
parenthesis myself slung without apparent motive into
the page of the world’s life, and the style is of the man,
you know —but the long and short of it is that I won t
leave enough to buy a clean sheet to be buried in. Not
that it matters to me, but the poor children ! Her s as
well as mine—don’t forget that.
Brothers that we are, love never, since the first years,
was lost between us. You thought when we came from
school I was favoured in everything—l was given every-
thing you coveted—and you had nothing, you said, but
what vou were pleased to term my leavings. You were
always vulgar, Robert. If you help my children 1
mean her’s—keep them free from that taint, I pray you.
Yes we hated one another ; I you from superciliousness
and pride because I was the favoured one; you me,
from envv. You always were a mean, jealous
cur, Robert. That is the reason you have been
successful, doubtless. It is so easy to be suc-
cessful when one runs away from the conscience ot a
gentleman. Even when I won her from you, your jealousy
generated a sharper malignity because with her I got
Karanuk out-station. I could appreciate a wholesome
hatred bee -use I won the woman upon whom you had set
your hopes, but a love if the woman complicated with
creek-frontages and lightly-timbered country I could not
and cannot understand.
However, that is all gone now—the money, and the
station and the love. Only the hatred remains— and the
children. T ,
Perhaps lam a fool to write this way ; perhaps not. n
you are disposed to help the children you will do so in
spite of this last bubbling forth of my contempt. And it
you will not help them, then, if beyond the Boundary
they know anything at all, I shall sunly be glad I did not
abandon that hate at the ebbing of the tide which I had
nourished at the flood.
. Do you see this stain ? The bleeding came

And the boy—he's come back and siys he received

PRICE WARUNG.


THE STOLEN SHEEP
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Say, mate, it’s a tidy long stretch since we
parted near old Lambin’ Flat;
Twenty years? - Yes, I reckon it is, and a
sprinklin’ of odd ’uns on that.
Let’s see—after you with the light—l’ve a fancy
’twas sometime about
A day or so after the time old Tommy the
butcher pegged out.
What ! don’t you remember old Tommy? Why
all on the diggin’s knew him—
Had a butchery down the main Creek, pretty
nigh to the Company’s whim ;
A small, wizened runt of a man like a shrivelled-
up scrap of green-hide—
I thought you would call him to mind—did they
ever find out how he died ?
No ? never got trace of his carcase from that day
to this. Umph—that’s queer,
But I fancy there’s one man about who could
soon make the mystery clear.
Foul play ?—h’m—well no, not exactly, that is
if it’s murder you mean ;
But as cheesey a case for a cor’ner as ever Ned
Tucker has seen.
P’rhaps now you would-
n’t believe that I
helped to put
Tommy away,
Oh, you needn’t be
starin’ like that
though you feel a
bit shocked, I dare
say.
But listen: It’s safe
now, I reckon, to
let the cat out of the
bag, £)
And I know you of yore
pretty well as a
fellow not given to
mag.
Well, you see ’twas on
old Possum Flat we
were workin’, Jim
Clinton and I
Both lime-burners right
from the start, so,
of course, it was
Root, hog —or die ;
But after a-bottomin’
duffers for five or
six weeks at a spell
We were not only down
at the mouth but
were down at the
heel, mate, as well.
Notascrap in the tucker-
bag left, and not
even the price of a
feed,
With our credit all stop-
ped at the stores
and no friend to be
found in our need,
And the hunger a-gnaw-
in’ our in’ards and
givin’ us rats all the day,
Though we pulled in our belts a bit tighter
a-thinkin’ to ease it that way.
So at last we got puzzlin’ our wits ’bout makin’
a rise in the dark ;
And of all other men we considered old
Tommy our easiest mark.
We didn’t half relish the notion, and there was,
you may safely depend,
A fight betwixt hunger and conscience, but
conscience gave up in the end.
So we laid both our noddles together and faked
up a dodge pretty slick
To wait on the butcher that evenin’ with an
order for mutton—on tick—
That’s to put a good face on it, matey. How*
somever, one part of our plan
Was to borrow a small-wheeled toboggan from
a cove that they called Curly Dan.
You recollect Dan, I suppose?—a short, stiff-
built style of a chap,
Who used to hawk cow-heel and tripe in a
queer little box of a trap.
Well, the dead of the night found us there with
the barrow all ready at hand.
And our hearts goin’ thumpity-thump, that
shaky we scarcely could stand.
y,r ° hadn’t much trouble in forcin’ and enterin’
into his den—
As you know, they weren’t ovcr-perticler about
their door-fastenin’s then—
So in I goes, clawin’ and gropin’, whilst Clinton
kept watch in the street,
And it seemed that each carcase and quarter
was wrapped in a calico sheet.
I didn’t stand pickin’ and choosin’ to get at the
best in the stall,
But made a wild grab for the nearest and
lugged it away, hook and all,
And staggerin’, half-starved as I was, with the
slippery load on my back,
I slung it down flop in the cart and we trundled
away down the track.
It was not till we reached the old tent that
we ventured to peep at our prize—
Y <dl, I ve seen some queer things in my travels,
but never a worse met my eyes.
Ugh ! it makes me feel all of a creep when I
think of the horrible sight.
‘ Jumpin’ Joss!” says I, “what have we here?’’
when a puff o’ wind blows out the light.
And there I stands shakin’ and quakin’, whilst
Jim was knocked all of a heap,
For the thing that we saw in the barrow was
not in the form of a sheep.
No ; but there in a greasy old night-shirt that
covered him down to his toes,
With a rope round his thin, stringy neck, and
all blue at the gills and the nose,
And his eyes like the eyes of a grasshopper, a*
startin’ right out of his head.
Lay the scraggy old scrubber himself, lad,
Wee Tommy, the butcher, stark dead.
There was nothin’ else for it, of course, but to
plant his old carcase and slide,
But as neither of us had the stummick to handle
his cold, clammy hide
We backed the cart up to a shaft, and just
tilted him in neck-and-crop
And never cried crack till we’d shovelled a
cartload of mullock on top.
Then we run the truck into the scrub, packed
our blueys and went on a tower,
“ Jumpin' Joss!" says I, “what have we here?”
And paid all onr debts wid the storekeeps at
the rate of about five mile an hour ;
But the devil knows how we’d have fared if we
hadn't met you on the way
And—well that was the end of old Tommy;
so here’s to his ashes I say !
Richmond, Vic.
TOM FREEMAN.
Some traveller reports that in some place
or other in Central Africa the legal tender consists
of old brass kettles melted down, and the local
half-crown weighs 4 lbs. Any Treasurer who
wants to put the finances on a solid basis might
have a look at this scheme. When a man is liable
to be killed by being hit on the stomach with two-
and-six the solidity of things is self-apparent.
Also, as a means for preventing the undue
accumulation of wealth, the currency in question
seems to have its advantages ; it would be a
restraint on the flying bank-clerk; for nobody
could lose his money in the street because he
would always feel so much lighter when he dropped
a coin, that he couldn’t help knowing it was gone.
Payable Gold.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I.— Concerning a Prisoner at the Bar.
There are two persons in New South WaLs, only
two of us, who know why there was not a broken
neck among the broken hearts in a fashionable
square of a large city, in Scotland some thirty or
forty years ago. The neck was not broken, but
would have been stretched, if a girl and I had
told all we knew. We were never asked, because
no one knew that we were in any way aware of
what took place in connection with a poisoning-
case that caused a great sensation. Not proven,
gentlemen; not proven, ladies ; not proven, all ye
common men; not proven, servant-maids and
factory-girls, who are spending your pennies on
Latest Editions; not proven, but very near it.
So we’ll let that flea stick to the wa’. And I'll
tell you how we met again and again and ye
again.
11.— Concerning a Lady at the Bar.
There were four of us in one claim on the
Cudgegong River diggings. We were a good lot,
that is, we didn’t quarrel, hardly ever argued, and
did not drink more than we could carry. It was
Friday night, and I was alone in the hut. My
mates had gone down the street, one to fetch a
sluicing-fork and two, each to carry a bag of chaff
for one old horse. I could hear them returning
and arguing earnestly and loudly, but apparently
about a trivial matter-the colour of something—
and old Throok. shouted, “We’ll leave it to Ralph
and he will decide. I pay for champagne if he
gives it red." Then they entered the hut and placed
a bottle of square-gin on the table. After a nip, I
said : “ Well, mates, what is the point at issue?”
“Just this,” said Dugald Little, “there’s a new
girl, or woman, at Driscoll’s, and I say her hair is
red, so does Spruhan, but Throck. says there is
not even a shade of auburn, and we want you,
to-morrow night, mind you, by lamp-light, to
state your opinion of her wig. But, by Jove, it’s
no false hair; it is the most beautiful I have ever
seen, red though it be.” Then I thought of the
old-gold hair of a head in Scotland, and of our
quarrel and parting on the night that the Jersey-
man drank coffee and arsenic, and, promising to
well and truly try the ease, I took a pull at the
square bottle, and slept the sleep of a ground-
si nicer.
Saturday. Knock off early; wash ; clean
clothes on—not flash ones—only clean moles and
Crimean shirt, Yankee hat and red sash ; boots
greased—not blackened—and down we go to
Driscoll’s. 8
Have you ever, at night, been dazed by a flash
of lightning? You can almost feel the blast. If
walking, it makes you
stop and raise your hand
in front of your eyes.
If standing, it makes you
start and wink. That
was my feeling on enter-
ing the bar, for there
stood my lady, once my
lady-love, and what she
was doing behind a bar
in a shanty on the dig-
gings was, and is, to me
a mystery. She knew
me and put one finger
on her lips before any-
one could see our mutual
recognition, and shook
hands when the introduc-
tion, “Miss Knox, Mr.
Leishman,” took place.
The fun on that Satur-
day night was fast and
furious. The new bar-
maid was drawing cus-
tom : some noisy men
had christened her “ the
Ked Flag ”; some quieter
ones had come to the
conclusion that this was
no ordinary drawer of
beer and brandy, and the
entry of our party caused
a “universal shout”
(Shakespeare). Then
Throck. spoke out thus :
“Miss Knox, we are
only diggers, all of us
men, some of us, I hope,
gentlemen. You must
excuse us for having
made a bet concerning
your personal appear-
ance. You must excuse
us if we even give
you a nick-name. Some
of us have ventured to
call you the ‘ Red Flag.’
I wish you were our ban-
ner of that colour. You’ll
know by and bye what
it means; but here we
have our quiet man, Mr.
Ralph Leishman, and he
is to decide what the
colour of your hair is, and
on that your cognomen
. will greatly 3epehd.
Ralph will name its colour and I shall name you
You are not angry ?” J
Then Miss Knox quietly said : “ Ralph, what is
the colour of my hair ? ”
, Old gold and Little and Spruhan ordered
the champagne.
Whilst it was being opened, Throck. disappeared
—soon to return with a bunch of pale-red ribbons
and a silver-arrow attached to a sort of hair-pin
Ihis he persuaded Miss Knox to allow him to
fasten in her hair (I admit the shocking taste
of the contrast), and calling up all the company
he proclaimed that he hereby hoisted the Red Flag
on Payable Gold ; and thereafter no one knew her
by any other name on the Cudgegong diggings.
And next week we struck it, to the tune of four
figures a man; like men we went home, Throck. to
Little and I to Scotland, and Spruhan
to But on that Saturday night as I
bought the inevitable key-brand-square, Miss
Knox slipped a note into my hand which I read
when I got a chance alone. This was it :
Ralph,—As a man you will not seek to renew our
acquaintance. As a gentleman you will make no inquiries
here or at linns concerning, Yours not as formerly
So I dined with her father and mother at acountry
place in Scotland, but never a word said they to
me of Pat. 1 fancy the old gentleman listened
with more than ordinary attention to my digging-
yarns, but he made no sign. b
lll.—Concerning a Lady at the German Bar.
Flood-time on the Palmer, Famine on one
side, plenty on the other. In one shanty, abeauti
ful female, laughing at the horseflesh and native
cabbage, and Jots of men round her proposing means
of communication, means which none had the
pluck to try. Then Mrs. Knox—just Mrs. from the
familiarity that raises your landlady to married
rank—looking askance, says quickly: “There
is one man among you who will swim if I ask
him.” Sol must needs answer: “There is one
man who will swim if you do not ask him.” Then
we go up the river, about a hundred yards above
where the packers are camped on the opposite
side. A stout fishing-line is made fast round my
chest and a knife tied to it in case of entangle-
ment. I dive in. It is the easiest way to start
The line is carefully paid out, so as not to sag and
belly too much in the current, and I arrive among
the packers, noting that Patricia has been watch-
ing me ere I go into a tent to dress. There is
soon a rope across and easy communication. That
night I was regaled on Queensland rum by every
man-Jack (why Jack ?) at the German Bar, and
still Miss Knox knew me not apparently.
From rush to rush, from the Palmer to the
Hodgkinson and to whatever shanties existed
went Payable Gold and her red flag.
One day I met her in Brisbane, shook hands and
asked her “ Where away ? ” One word for answer
“Silverton.” Then to continue: “Are you
making money ? ” There was a slight pause, and
slow the reply came: “I have made money
Good morning.” Then Pat. came to the great
rush at Painted Rocks, which brings me to our last
meeting.
IV.—Concerning the Raising op the Bar.
I had given up mining, broke, gone, stiff as a
crutch, to use the slang of to-day. I was, in fact
boundary-rider on a station and had taken the
horse Auricomus to show for my boss at the
Merriwarrie Agricultural, &c. The chestnut was
entered as a hack and as a lady’s hunter. There
was great jealousy between my employer, Tom
Creagh, and another squatter, a Mr. Flux. Some
hundreds were bet as to which would win, Auri-
comus or Medium, Men will bet about anything;
the prize was only a bit of ribbon and a couple of
sovereigns. Young Flux had got Mrs, Nectar
the crack horsewoman, to ride his nag and Mr!
Creagh had the promise of Miss Tigerly to show off
Aury, ” But this young lady was stricken by the
particular form of typhoid for which Merriwarrie
has obtained a just celebrity, and we (Tom, I, and
Auricomus) were on our beam-ends. No duffer
would do for our mount. He was not rusty or
nasty, but terribly flash, and that made the boss
and me drink gin and beer and rum and others of
that ilk, until Tom had reached the crying stage
of ebriety, But wine, though a mocker, is an
inspiration in many cases. The name Auricomus,
the colour of the horse, and the square-key-brand
brought Patricia into my head. Now if any woman
can ride, she can. Painted Rocks are only twenty
miles from Merriwarrie. Mr. Creagh’s horses are
fast, and fast enough for me at 7 a.rn., in front of
the Red Flag Hotel at the Rocks. “ A word with
you, Mrs. Knox, in private ?”
In the bar-parlour, Patricia took the initiative.
‘No love now, Ralph; no nonsense. Business if
you will, and not too much of that.”
Well, here was a fix. I wanted to work on the
old flame, although it was extinct, and I was de-
termined to carry my point. Therefore, I did a
mean thing : I worked on her gratitude ; in fact,
demanded a quid pro quo for my silence during
these long years, and this was the outcome of my
meanness :
“ Patricia,! have kept your secret; have never
made enquiries as to the why and the wherefore of
your disguise and elopement, and in return I beg
you to give me this day. Hear me out. These
ponies at the door belong to a man who is my
master and my friend. He has a horse at Merri-
warrie to be ridden, just for ten minutes, by a
lady. His promised rider, you know, Miss
Tigerly; she is down with fever, and the horse
Auricomus—called by me after your golden hair—
wants a thorough rider—no duffer—and, above
all, one with hands and a figure. Will you come
with me in the buggy ? We can drive over in two
hours, arriving about 10 or 12. You can buy a
tall hat and gossamer veil; no one need know
you. At two o’clock you can drop as from the
clouds, an angel in a habit and bell-topper, and
you can win this prize for my boss, who has been
so good to me. Will you ? I will never ask you
for any other favour, although I will do for you
as much as I would have done years ago. Answer
me.”
There was no hesitation. “ Get breakfast; wait
for me after in the buggy.”
We never spoke during that two hours’ drive.
In Merriwarrie, Flux was jubilant, chaffing Tom
Creagh. The latter was satisfied that I had not
taken the buggy for nothing. When we drove up
to Bellarbo’s Hotel he stood on the steps; he was
sober, and winked—not at Pat., but me. Then,
turning to Flux, he said : “Double all my bets.”
“Done.” Again—“ Any more money to lose,
Flux, old fellow?” But Flux said, “Not a
stiver.”
Two p.m. The horses are paraded. At a glance
there were none in it but Medium and Auricomus,
the latter for choice, as soon as the public saw the
splendid figure, riding and coolness of Mrs. Knox.
Funny that no one recognised her. Hundreds
had seen her at the Painted Rocks; many only
the day before. But the judges soon decided
Auncomus, 1; Medium, highly commended. Now
the jumping-bar was near the gate, and between
it and the regular exit a crowd of yahoos were
fiddling with its fixtures. They had raised it to
about five-feet-six. The riding-track was clear
but for the bar when “ Whoop! look out, she’s
coming again; ” and down the tan came Aury and
Pat., not a runaway, but just at a hand-gallop;
what the niggers call 12 annas to the rupee, the
latter being full speed. Over they go; out at the
gate, and when I walked up to Beflarbo’s, Payable
Gold had gone off in the coach for Painted Rocks.
Ladies and gentlemen, females and persons,
harridans and cads, that is all I promised to tell
you. But, _ like Bobby Salmon, the ginger-bread
man of Kircaldy, I’ll give you another short
chapter gratis, for luck.
V.— Concerning Mt Bar.
As I live, I know not why Patricia left her
home. Her friends thought it was to follow me,
but my appearance, after my rise, at the Cudge-
gong dispelled that idea. However, the Red
Flag is now kept by Mr. and Mrs. Ralph Leish-
man, where the best, &c., &c.
And in the Red Flag is my claim, my claimant,
my wife, my life, unalloyed, pure and precious,
Payable Gold.
SCOTTY THE WRINKLER.


The Row in our Boarding-
House.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The trouble began on the night when a
newly-imported British youth named Johnson
appeared at our boarding-house.
There were ten of us there before his arrival,
including Bern, the humorous Polish tailor, w’ o
was vaguely understood to have thrown bombs at
all the royal families of Europe, and then gone
into exile. We paid seventeen shillings a week
each, not including washing; and we lived
riotously on boiled mutton. There were more
empty beer - bottles in the bedrooms, and
more laughter, and more grease slopped on
the floor, and the candle-ends got into the
soup oftener in that boarding-house than in
any other I ever heard of. Also, the neighbours
got less sleep than anybody ever did in the
vicinity of any other boarding-house. The dining-
room had not been papered since the beginning of
history, and the landlady had only one eye ; also,
her daughter had recently eloped with a non-
union printer. She, the landlady, was aged about
40, and wore a green dress, and in the evenings
she used to sing songs to us with her hair down.
These few details will convey a reasonably good
idea of the nature of that wild Bohemian estab-
lishment.
One windy evening, in March, the landlady had
agitated the bell on the stairs, as was her custom.
Her weapon was a sort of cowbell, and when she
wrestled with it on the murky staircase she
looked like a witch dancing on a heath. Her
arms, her hair, her feet, her green dress, and her
trodden-down shoes flew in eight different direc-
tions, and her one eye and the bell flew in two
more. Strangers coming down in the dark, and
meeting this apparition suddenly, generally took
her for a heap of excited boa-constrictor, or an
immense octopus leaping on the top step. Poor,
old, agitated female—she is dead now. She
broke her neck in the passage one day rushing
down to look at a funeral. But if she had kept
on ringing that dinner-bell she would have been
immortal. Death couldn’t have aimed straight
enough to hit her in her gambols.
I rushed down to the dining-room at the first
signal, and meeting Bern and two more coming
tumultuously in the opposite direction, we got
jammed in the doorway. I was just going to pass
some uncomplimentary observations when we all
caught sight of a spectacle such as the oldest in-
dividual in that boarding-house had never
seen before. A great calm descended upon us,
and we disentangled ourselves and went in
silently.-
What I saw was an object like a naked infant’s
hind-leg, resting in a careless, graceful attitude
against a chair. There was a bracelet on it, and
attached to one end of it was a woman. She was
attired in a silk dress which exposed her right
down to the fifth knob of her spine, or there-
abouts, and she had a necklace, and an eye-glass,
and sundry rings. There was a frozen expression
in her eye—a look of cold derision that seemed to
fall like a curse upon the whole company. This
was Johnson’s wife. Johnson himself was there in
a tail-coat, and a tremendous collar, and another
eye-glass, and he had a silver bangle on his wrist.
He was the first male human being that I ever
saw inside a bangle, and I am prepared to swear
that he was the very first who ever wore a bangle
in a boarding-house.
Between them they made just one remark all
dinner-time. It was “ Haw ! ” I could have said
the same thing myself if I had been dead.
We did not eat much that evening, and there
was very little conversation. We were all para-
lysed by the spectacle of Johnson and his wife.
They kept looking in a pensive, perplexed sort of
way round the table as if they were searching for
some of the commonest necessaries of life, such as
were to be found in stacks in their ancestral castle;
and then they would wake up as if from a dream,
and recollect suddenly that they were castaways
in a savage land, where the wild aborigines never
heard of the article, whatever it might be. And
when they were finished the lady went and smote
the piano with an arm of might for about 38
minutes, after which the pair retired and were
seen no more that night.
That was the beginning of the row in our
boarding-house.
# * *
Next morning the owner of the establish-
ment came down early, and refreshed herself with
a few melodies before breakfast. She was a
strange, promiscuous, half-savage female, and was
wont at times to get up before daybreak, and thud
out all manner of lost chords on the keyboard,
and then she would keep time with her slippers,
and her head, and whirl her tangled locks in the
air, and cast the tails of her dressing gown out
behind her in a frenzy of inspiration. After that
she would scuttle away with a prodigious clatter,
and clutch the sausages that were to be fried for
breakfast, and for about half-an-hour the air
would be darkened with a chaos of food and
dishes, and it would blow a gale of gravy, cruets,
loaves, and similar properties. When she entered
with the breakfast-tray it always made me think
of Napoleon’s commissariat department flying
from Moscow, for she generally arrived at a
gallop, shoving one-half the provisions in front of
her and dragging the other half behind, and his-
sing to scare off the cat, which ran with its tail
up in the rear of the procession, trying vainly to
claw at the alleged eatables.
Then she would fling herself on the bell like a
hash-house keeper possessed, and make a riot that
was calculated to wake the lost souls of all the
dead boarders who had shaken off this mortal coil,
and were eating spectral ham and eggs in the
fields of Asphodel. On this morning I found her
leaping and gambolling on the stairs as usual, and
I stopped to propound a solemn question,
“Mrs. Jones,” I said sternly, “who are the
partially dressed intruder in the bed-furniture,
and the tailor’s advertisement with the jewellery
on his fore-leg ?”
“ He’s in the gas-office,” she replied in gasps, as
she threw herself up against the air with the bell.
“ Newly-married ! He’s English, and got £BO a
year! I took them in at a reduced rate. ”
“Oh, you did ! Now, look here; have you any
reason to suppose he’s a duke or an emperor, or
anything of the sort ?”
“No !”
“Do you suppose, as a respectable Christian
woman, that he’s got a castle anywhere ?”
“ No, I don’t s’pose he has.”
“Did he come in with the Conqueror, now ?”
“ There wasn’t anybody with him when he came
in, that I saw.”
“And did you know when you took them in
that she was going to stick so far out of her
clothes ?”
“ No, I didn’t. If I'd knowed it I’d have
thrown her out.”
I went into breakfast in a subdued mood.
Johnson was there looking with a shocked ex-
pression at the sausages, and shuddering in a new
bangle and a collar that was six sizes taller than
the previous one. His wife, with a fresh bracelet,
was glaring at the same viand, and the expression
in her eye seemed to say “ Where am I; is this a
horrid dream ?” It was the only morning, too, in
six months when the sausages hadn’t either fallen
under the grate or been dropped on the stairs,
and this made their conduct all the more uncalled-
for.
At lunch time Johnson’s wife appeared in
another fresh bracelet, and with a profusion of
jewellery on her right forefinger. And as for
Johnson, astounding as it may seem, he had a
third bangle on. Evidently he had one for each
meal, and this was his lunch-bangle.
In the evening the lady had another silk dress
on, and it exposed one knob more of her spine
than the previous one. Johnson wore his second
clean collar and his dinner-bangle, and his small
countenance, looming over his great expanse of
shirt-front, looked like a pallid bird of prey on the
top of an icy mountain. They talked together
in scraps of inferior French, and when the meal
was over Mrs. Johnson again smote the piano, and
then retired coldly to her own apartments.
That night we held an indignation meeting on
the balcony. The landlady had deposited the
vegetable-dish out therethrough the window when
she was getting ready to make her turbulent entry
with the pudding, and had forgotten it. Inside
there were two candles burning, and one lamp
with a broken shade—the gas had been cut off for
non-payment. The bread was in a plate on top of
the piano, and the dishes were in an unwashed
condition downstairs, and the mistress of the
house was singing a shrill melody in the kitchen.
And among these surroundings Bern, the Polish
“ He entered at that moment .”
tailor, passed a resolution that he would drive
Johnson off the premises, or die in the attempt.
After that our souls felt relieved, and we settled
down to placid harmony. Somebody found some
liquor in a bottle under his bed and brought it
down. The cards were fished out, and we played
nap, and gave each other 1.0.U.’s on a lavish
scale. The lamp flickered and went out with an
awful smell. Then one of the candles died amid
a tremendous blob of grease, and we gave up
playing, and smoked in the semi-darkness. The
bread cast a long, gaunt shadow on top of the
piano, and the vegetable-dish was forgotten on the
balcony. And finally, our landlady burst in with
the green dress partially open at the back, and
wearing a hat decorated with six broken feathers ;
and fell over the cat in the doorway; and she
played some tune that was all discords, on the
piano, while Bern sang a dirge about some for-
gotten period when he lived beside a river, and
went out musing with a female by moonlight. He
was so moved by the recollection that he wept into
the last candle, and it went out also, leaving us in
darkness.
# * *
I woke on that third morning with a
vague, indefinable feeling that somebody had died
in the night. There was a Sabbath calm brooding
over Jones’s boarding-house which was quite
foreign to that clamorous establishment, and as I
listened it suddenly occurred to me that Bern
must have carried out his threat, and dynamited
Johnson in the darkness. All the doors seemed
to close softly, instead of shutting with a soul-
destroying bang as usual. The landlady flopped
about gently on her trodden-down shoes, instead
of bringing in the breakfast with a crash and a
shriek, and shedding dishes about in all direc-
tions ; and she rang the bell gently, whereas she
usually rang it like a dray-load of escaped lunatics.
I descended the stairs amid a prevailing smell of
ham and eggs, and went into the dining-room.
Johnson was breakfasting with a troubled ex-
pression and a bangle, and his wife was glaring
across the table. Opposite them Bern was solemnly
feeding in evening dress. He looked absolutely
vacant, and seemed to be lost in profound thought,
and the frantic excitement of the other eight
boarders made no impression on him at all. He
wore a pair of kid gloves and a bracelet, and his
moustache was elegantly waxed. Johnson rose at
last, and walked round this apparition, but the
apparition never moved. Finally the Pole got up,
and thoughtfully fished an ancient, battered straw
hat from under his chair, and having jammed it
hard down on his head, he went out of the room
and out of the house like a man who was lost in
profound vacancy.
Nobody spoke. A species of paralysis seemed
to have descended on that boarding-house. But,
after a moment, Johnson rushed on to the balcony
and glared in a cataleptic fashion after this un-
accountable visitant. And outside, on the stairs,
stood a petrified landlady in a smudged green
dress, and with a hat with six broken feathers in
it, cocked rakishly over one eye, and two misfit
shoes that seemed to have taken root in the floor.
She held a teapot in one hand and a pie in the
other, and she was unconsciously pouring the tea
into the pie, and the pie on to the staircase.
» # #
Bern turned up in evening costume again
at lunch-time, but, though expectation rose to
fever-heat, nothing happened. Owing to mental
strain, however, the landlady fainted in the sink
in the course of the afternoon.
At dinner, the Johnsons began to show signs of
wear and tear. Mrs. Johnson’s left eyebrow was
out of line, and her complexion had shifted round
to her ear. Evidently it had been put on with a
reckless hand, while the wearer was under the
influence of considerable excitement. Her husband
had an enraged and harassed look, and his hair
was off the straight, and his tie was crumpled.
The landlady’s shoes were in the hall, from which i
gathered that shehadlost them in her agitation, and
her hat was under the table. As for that joyous
Bohemian herself, she was dodging round in a
semi-hysterical condition, and when I entered
she had just given Johnson the whole dish of
potatoes, and had shoved the entire joint on
another boarder’s plate, after which she deposited
the kettle in an abstracted manner on the table-
cloth. Then she fled unaccountably round the
table and supplied us all with a number of other
remarkable things too numerous and too badly
assorted to mention. There was a dead and awful
silence in the company—a silence that was too
awful to be accounted for on the supposition that
Bern had appeared again in evening-dress and
caused a panic. For one awful moment the.
thought occurred to me that the insane Pole
might possibly have appeared clothed in a marked
scarcity of dress of any kind
He entered at that moment, carefully removing a
shiny bell-topper as he came in, and depositing a
pair of gloves in its inmost recesses. He was
attired in grey pants and a linen jacket, but
he had taken the sleeves out of the jacket and
out of his shirt, and he was “ cut low ” at the
neck. Also he wore a gold bracelet—in fact, he
was a remarkably good copy of Johnson’s wife.
The landlady uttered a shriek when she saw him,
and disappeared down the stairs. The cat escaped
up the chimney, scattering last night’s dead ashes
out of the grate as it went. The other boarders
choked with insane glee, but Bern only contem-
plated them for an instant with a lack-lustre eye
and a funereal visage, and sat down. He was the
one unmoved individual in the company.
Then Johnson arose, and shaking a fork in the
air with a palsied hand, he made a brief oration.
“ I give notice,” he shrieked, “ that I will leave
this den of infamy at the end of this week. lam
accustomed —I mean, I am not accustomed —
British constitution—degraded parody upon a
human being—l refuse to associate—l—dash and
onfound the—the—objectionable ruffian opposite
—insolent outrage upon all gentlemanly senti-
ment —gorilla on the other side of the table ”
t He gave out there, and choked. With a wild
howl ho heaved up the table and capsized the
ruins of it bodily at Bern, and then he tramped
heavily over the debris and engaged his enemy in
mortal combat. The Pole emerged from the
wreckage, and with his hair full of gravy, and
corned beef in both ears, and one eye stopped up
with cabbage, and mustard streaming down his
back, he flew at Johnson like a wild cat. The
candles went out; Mrs. Johnson shrieked in the
passage; the landlady wailed dismally in the
kitchen ; and all was horror and confusion.
* * *
There was an item in Johnson’s bill when
he left, which read ; “Broken crockery, 225. Od.”
I believe he hypothecated his lunch-bangle to pay
it. The gas has been turned on again since then,
and the landlady sings weird songs to us in the
gloaming, with her hair down as of old. She
wears the same green dress, and drops her shoes
all over the house as before, but it isn’t the same
landlady—the old one broke her neck on the stairs
as beforementioned, and her daughter has in-
herited her clothes and the business, and she rings
the dinner-bell with even more vim than her
lamented predecessor. She reminds me very much
of her mother, for she drops the meals on the stairs
in a way that calls up sad, pathetic memories ; and
rushes in as recklessly holding out the gravy in
front of her, and rushes out again as madly to
look for the vegetables, as her deceased relative
could have done. Johnson is in gaol somewhere—
there was a gap in his accounts which even his
jewellery could not fill; and his wife has eloped
with our new landlady's husband, the printer.
Also, nobody wears evening-dress in our boarding-
house any more.
TITUS SALT.
The Australian Desert: A Dream of
the Future.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Shunned and dreaded ; untrodden, drear ;
A realm of hunger, of thirst, of fear,
The desert heart of Australia lies,
An iron land, beneath brazen skies.
Where a dewless morn greets the summer days,
And the wide plains loom through a trembling
haze;
Where -with calm, still lakelets, ’neath shadowy
rocks,
The delusive mirage the wanderer mocks.
Where the pools of rain that fall fresh and sweet,
Turn salt ’ere they dry in the blistering heat.
Where the hot winds roam over scrub and sand ;
And the sun shines down on a lifeless land.
There, the passionless stars long vigils keep
O’er a silent waste, in a dreamless sleep.
Not even the wild dog’s cry is heard,
Nor the wailing hoot of a flitting bird.
At times, gaunt, meagre figures pass
Through the mulga scrub and the prickly grass,
Searching for roots ere the day expire,
Then, huddle close round the flickering fire.
Hungry and thirsty, at break of day
Rise, like black shadows, and stalk away.
Desert! You keep your secrets well
Of the gallant hearts who failed and fell;
You hold their bones in a close embrace,
You make no sign, and they left no trace.
Unrelenting, dire, in your sternest mood,
You watched them die in your solitude,
Your burning breast was their lonely bed ;
In the unknown land sleep the long-lost dead.
The time draws near when the low, bare hills
Will echo the songs of a thousand rills.
Deep down in the beds of the old-world streams,
They sleep unheeded, and dream their dreams ;
Till the magic drill bids them wake again,
And they rise to water the thirsty plain.
The whispering stalks of the maize grow higher;
Dor the scrub has fallen ’neath axe and fire.
Where the spinifex grew, ’midst the sandstone
rooks,
Browse the lowing herds, and the bleating flocks ;
And the sun now shines on the peaceful scene
Of farm,* and orchard, and vineyard green.
The iron horse has bridged the space
That only the camel before dared face.
Rippling downward in limpid waves,
The waters well from the sunless caves,
Till the bare lagoons, sun-baked and dried,
Are filled to the brim with the flowing tide.
And children play in the grassy glade
Where the lost explorers’ bones are laid.
ERNEST FAVENG.
The Hawthorn.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By the road, near her father’s dwelling,
There groweth a hawthorn tree ;
Its blossoms are fair, and fragrant,
As the love that I cast from me.
It is all abloom this morning,
In the sunny silentness,
And glows by the roadside, radiant
As a bride in her bridal dress.
But, ah me ! at sight of its blossoms
No pleasant memories start;
I see but the thorns beneath them'—
And the thorns they pierce my heart.
V. J. DALEY.

CADBURY’S COCOA is closely allied to
milk in the large proportion of flesh-forming and
strength-sustaining elements that it contains. It is pre-
pared on the principle of excluding the superabundance
of fatty indigestible matter with which Cocoa abounds
—supplying a refined thin infusion of absolutely pure
Cocoa, exhilarating and refreshing, for Breakfast, Lun-
cheon, Tea or Supper, giving staying power and im-
parting new life and vigour to growing children, and
those of delicate constitutions.
Kelly’s Conversion.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]

While his hot breath thick with the taint of beer,
Came forth from his lips in a drunken jeer,
SriU'ed h im ,
And ekd in grey, one and all drf pray
Then the captain left him in mild despair,
But before the music took up its blare
A pale-faced lassie stepped out and spoke—
A sad little girl in a sad grey cloak —
“ Rise up, Kelly, your work’s to do,
Kelly, the Saviour’s a-calling you. ’_
He strove to look wise, rubbed at his eyes, _
Looked down at the earth, looked up at the skies,
And something that p’raps was his conscience
He seemed’perplex’d as again he heard
The girl with the garments of saddest hue
Say, “Kelly, the Saviour’s a calling you.”
• • • • • • •
He got on his knees and thence to his feet,
And stumbled away down the dustv street;
Contrived to cadge at the “ pub.” a drink,
But still in his ear the glasses chink
And jingle out to the one refrain,
Clear as the lassie’s voice again :
“Kelly, Kelly, come here to me.
Kelly the Eager, I ve work for thee
He trembled, and dropped the tumbler, and slopped
The beer on the counter the barman stopped,
With a curious eye on his haggard face,
Kelly, old fellow, you re going the pace,
Don t you fancy it s time to take
A pull on yourself, put your foot on the brake ?
You 11 have the horrors, without a doubt.
This time next week, if you don t look out.
But he didn t he sobered himself that night.
And “ that time next week ’’ he was nearly right,
Yet still at the mill, tho he d stopped the grog,
As the saw bit into the green pine log,
The wood shrieked out to him in its pain,
A fragment caught up the one refrain,
As the swift teeth cut and the sawdust flew—
“ Kelly, Kelly, I’ve work for you.”
Then the seasons fell and the floods came down
And laid the dust in the silent town;
No more the beat of hoofs and feet
Was heard the length of the crooked street,
For, leaving counter and desk and till,
All had fled to the far sandhill;
But everywhere that a man might dare
Risk life to save it, Kelly was there.
No more the voice had a tale to tell,
He’d found his work and he did it well—
Who stripped leggings and hat and coat
To swim the lagoon and procure the boat?
Who pushed out in the dead of night
At the mute appeal of a beacon-light?
Who was blessed by the women then,
And who was cheered by the stalwart men,
As he shot the rapids above the town
With two pale Smiths and a weeping Brown,
Landing them safe from his cockleshell,
Woefully frightened, but safe and well,
WRh their friends on the sandhill all secure-
Who but Kellj you may be sure ?
They reckoned the heads up, one by one,
And be sighed as he thought that the work was
done.
But soon found out that ’twas not begun;
They counted away till it came to pass
They missed the little Salvation lass;
She’d been to pray with a man who lay
Sick on the river-shore, far away.
Men looked askance and the women smote
Their hands in grief, as he launched the boat,
And said, as he cast the painter loose :
“ Who’ll make another ? It’s little use
My going alone, for I’m nearly done,
And from here to the point is a stiffish run.”
Then one stepped forward and took an oar,
And the boat shot out for the other shore.
To and fro, where the gums hang low
And bar their passage, the comrades row
Hard, up-stream, where the waters race,
Steady, where floating branches lace,
Though many a danger and quick escape
And catch of breath, as the timbers scrape
_And thrill to the touch of some river shape,
Till at last the huts on the point draw near,
And over their shoulders the comrades peer.
The flood was running from door to door,
Two feet six on the earthen floor,
Half-way up to the bed it ran,
Where two pale women and one sick man
Crouched, and looked at the water’s rise,
With horror set in their staring eyes,
While the children wept, as the water crept,
But how the blood to their hearts high leapt
As over the threshold the rescuers stepped,
And, wrapped in blanket and shawl and coat,
Carried the saved to the crazy boat.
Then Kelly circled the little lass
With his strong right arm, and as in a glass
Saw himself in her eyes that shone
Sweet, in a face that was drawn and wan,
And he felt that for her life he’d give his own ;
Too short a moment her cheek was pressed
Close to the beat of his spray-wet breast,
While her hair just lay like a golden ray,
The last farewell of a passing day.
Gently he settled her down in the stern
With a tender smile, and had time to turn
To look to the others, and then he saw
That the craft was full and could hold no more.
He looked at the party, old, young and sick,
While he had no tie, neither wife nor chick.
Then—with a shove he sent out the boat
Far on the turbid stream afloat.
“ Pull !” said Kelly. “Now pull,” said he,
“ Pull with your load and come back for me,
You may be late, but at any rate
I’m better able than you to wait.”
They pulled and, looking back, saw him stand
Shading his eyes with his big, rough hand—
Silent, patient, and smiling-faced,
With the water curling around his waist.
Return they did, but they found him not,
Nought but the chimney then marked the spot;
They found him not when the boat went back,
Never a trace of him, never a track,
Only the sigh and the dreary cry
Of the gums that had wept to see him die:
These alone had a tale to tell
Of a life that had ended passing well—
The sad refrain of a hero’s fate
Tuned in a tongue we may not translate.
Facing Death with a bold, brave heart,
Chosing the nobler and better part;
Home to the land of eternal sun
Kelly had gone, for his work was done.
SURCINGLE.
An Idyll of the Season.
(WELL-SEASONED.)
I held her to my breast
In a vyce,
And was numbered with the blest
(She was nice).
Her responsive squeeze I felt—
Ah ! in Paradise we dwelt;
We were warm enough to melt
Tons of ice.
I told her of my love
’Twixt our grips :
There was mistletoe above,
And our lips
In a tender fashion met;
’Twas with honey hers were wet,
And this infant took, you Ltt I
Many sips.
We gave no heed to time,
No, nor place;
Oh ! what ecstasy sublime
To embrace !
I saw her eyelids close
As I stole her yellow rose,
For I envied its repose
’Midst the lace.
“ Are you married ?” quoth the maid,
With a wink;
Through the floor I felt afraid
I should sink.
But the truth I soon confess’t,
And she murmured on my chest,
“ Dear, the married ones are best,
Don’t you think ?
“ For they spoon and never tell
Other chaps.”
So I answered, “ Darling, well,
Yes, perhaps
You can trust them, as you say,
In a quiet sort of way,
And be happy while ye may—
On their laps.”
Then I took her on my knee—
Further bliss !
It was Christmastime, you see,
Same as this;
And I said, as fellows do,
“ When I’ve buried You-know-who,
Will my pet be Number Two ?”
(Answer —kiss).
Then the Missis came along
On the sly;
I had said she “ wasn’t strong ”
What a lie !
She gave that maid a shake
Which, I’m certain, made her ache,
And I had to get some steak
For my eye.
Thus the maiden and the bard
Were dispers’t ;
From my “second” I’m debarred
By my ‘ 1 first. ”
Though the married men are best,
As my vanished love confess’t,
I have heard my wife protest
“ They’re the worst.”

FEDERATION OR NO FEDERATION.
The latest scheme for Federation
Claims to be for the country’s weal,
While all may prove but botheration,
Which can’t he said of Pemell’s Meal.
His Golden Meal is what I mean,
’Tis good and pure and cheap I ween.
To Messrs. Colemane and Sons, Coota-
mundra.—Gentlemen,—Having received much
benefit from the use of your Victory Ointment
and Volatile Eucalypte, I can, with confidence,
recommend to all who suffer from skin diseases
the use of same. During the past nine years I
suffered from a most annoying skin disease on my
face, caused by the bite of a fly, and 'though the
most eminent doctors in this colony treated me
for the complaint, it gained ground and threatened
to unfit me for business. Acting on the advice of
a friend, I determined to try your Victory Oint-
ment and Volatile Eucalypte; the result has been
most successful. In cases of bronchitis and colds
I have used your Volatile Eucalypte with success,
and in a case which I met in the bush far away
from medical aid, it saved a life.—l am, gentle-
men, &c., D. M. Campbell. Cootamundra,
July 1, 188(1.

“Christmas, 1891.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A " Merry Christmas !” Aye, the bells are pealing
With joyous clangs of gladness and surprise ;
The light of Peace to broken hearts revealing,
The light of Love to sorrow-laden eyes.
A Merry Christmas ! spread abroad the tidings
Through all their channels let Joy’s streamlets J nice
Till the swift currents’ meetings and dividings
Drown all with joy too deep for utterance.
Yea, let us echo it o'er earth and ocean,
Till earth and sea with joy-pants heave and thrill,
Echoing with all their deep souls’deep emotion,
“ Peace upon earth, and unto men good-will! ”
Joy ! joy ! the end of human strife and weeping—
From each sad eye He wipes the tears for aye ;
The whole wide universe in peace is sleeping.
Let us be merry ; Christ was born to-day.
So, let us bear our joyous Christmas greeting
To brother and to sister, one by one,
And welcome them, while hands and lips are meeting,
With gladsome tidings of the victory won.
And first to you—dim eyes, back bent with labour,
Dull brain, toil-coarsened heart, toil-hardened hand,
United and warped—how is it with you, neighbour?
I fear me that you do not understand.
What, though you earn your crust with bitter toiling,
The common lot of all your common clay—
t)f light, of life, of love your souls despoiling?
Tis nothing ; think that Christ was born to-day.
God willed that you should be a slave, a chattel,
Of him who owns you, as he “owns” your soil,
Less valued than his meanest, lowliest cattle—
So, Merry Christmas to you, man of toil.
Next you, whose straitened brow and furtive glances
Proclaim the man at war with all his kind ;
Whose life is one long strife with risks and chances,
A gaol in front, an iron fate behind.
Horn thief, born homicide, born but to perish,
Fixed type, that sways.or alters not with time—
Your stunted soul the news will gladly cherish,
So, Merry Christmas to you, man of crime !
Next, haggard, drunken ; you that “ should he woman,’
Unwomanlike, besmirched with maudlin tears,
Bearing to-day your monstrous cross inhuman,
Unchanged for all the nineteen-hundred years
Since Christ bore His; go, buy your bread with sinning,
Sell to unlovely lust your kisses brute ;
Foredoomed to shame before your life’s beginning—
A Merry Christmas to you—Prostitute !
Enough, enough. Thus much of shame and sorrow,
Thus much, and more than tongue or pen can tell,
Is with us ; and we know not if a morrow
Shall dawn, whose sun shall chase the reek of hell.
Loud ring the joy-bells, jubilant and cheering ;
Half-drowned I hear men’s curses, women’s tears ;
So those blythe bells to my distempered hearing
Sound less like angels’ laugh them devils’ sneers.
Molong, N.S. Wales.
PAX VORISCUM.
Christmas Shopping and other Matters
Such as desire to see an ingenious present-
ment of Gnome-laud, we would strongly advise
to call in at Mr. Leon Harris’ jevvel-sl.op, Market-
street, next door to Hunter’s Boot Palace. The
picture is realistic. The quaint little miners are
hard at work smiting the mountain with their
hatchets, eager to secure the gems, which, ignis
fatuus- like, glance in and out among the rocks.
This clever design embellishes the window, but,
indoors, Mr. Harris can show a beautiful collection
of precious stones, both native and foreign, and
his artistic method of setting and clever groupings
of the different stones is above all praise. Old-
fashioned jewellery in his hands becomes trans-
formed into a thing of beauty. It is only fair to
add that Mr. Harris’ prices are as modest as his
work is first-rate.
* * *
Who does not enjoy an hour in Dymock’s
Book Arcade in George-street ? Here the book-
worms are to be seen at all times of the day,
fondly handling the beloved volumes, and making
their purchases according to the capacity of their
purses. It is a foregone conclusion that if a
certain book cannot be obtained at Dymock’s, it
is useless to try for it anywhere else in Sydney.
This firm is also showing a splendid assortment
of toys this year, the large showroom upstairs
being replete with all the latest novelties in the
way of mechanical toys, speaking dolls, silent and
sleeping dolls—in fact, everything the childish
heart can desire is to be found at Dymock’s.
• » *
Now that the summer is fairly upon us,
“ Macfie’s Cold Water Soap ” should claim a place
in every laundry. It possesses manifold virtues,
but the greatest is its power to cleanse clothes
without the aid of heat. When it is used in-
telligently, according to the instructions on each
tin, no boiling is necessary, therefore, there is a
saving both of fuel and discomfort, and the poor
laundress is no longer under the necessity of first
steaming herself over the hot suds, then roasting
herself over the copper fire. And, apart from
these considerations, by using Macfie’s Cold Water
Soap, it has been proved that the clothes are not
rotted as by using soda and common soap. It
has also the effect of whitening and softening the
hands, and why should not a laundress enjoy soft,
white hands ? Therefore, remember Macfie’s Cold
Water Soap.
# # *
Some of the prettiest and most appropriate
Christmas cards for sending Home this year are
those made by Mrs. Clarence White from the
Australian wild flowers. Great care is needed in
preparing these flowers so that the colours are
preserved, and Mrs. White has made quite a
success of her undertaking.
It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Go’s. “Cascade”
80 & 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is just
the place; it is certainly the largest and best
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*
COLEMANE & SONS, COOTAMUNDRA.
Dear Sirs, —One of your travelling agents called
on me some time ago, and I bought from him a
few bottles of your Eucalypte, and also a few
boxes of your ointment. Your agent having
convinced me that it was a medicine of no mean
order, I began by trying its efficiency on a poor
woman that has been suffering a long time (nearly
two years) with an abscess on the side of her nose,
so small that you cannot perceive it, but you can
feel that it is about the size of a pea just under the
eye. Sometimes it breaks out, and runs through
the nose. She had tried all the doctors in Goul-
burn, and they failed to do her any good. One
said it was rheumatism ; another said the bone
was beginning to decay; and the third doctor
said it was an abscess. She had used two boxes of
your ointment, and two bottles of the Eucalypte,
and says that it has done her more good than all
the doctors. Yours, etc.,
S. BROWN. Lake Bathurst.
TO MESSRS. COLEMANE AND SONS,
COOTAMUNDRA.
Gentlemen.—Having received much benefit
from the use of your Victory Ointment and
Volatile Eucalypte, I can with confidence
recommend it to all who suffer from Piles, either
blind or bleeding, as I have been using it since
Christmas myself and I find it has done me more
good than anything that I have tried for many
years. I have been a great sufferer from botti
kinds, and I have tried several doctors and none
of them have given me anything to do them good,
but your Victory Ointment and Eucalypte have
been most successful in easing the pain and
checking the bleeding. I also find it a beautiful
thing in cases of cold and fog-fever, as I have
tried it with good results on my own children.
You can make whatever use you like of this for
the benefit of the public.—l am, Sirs, Yours,
faithfully, Mrs. H. Weeks, Jerrawa, 18th Feb.,
1888.

Head —‘ A Golden Shanty.’
Asked what he thought of the N.S.W.
Womanhood Suffrage League, Major Rae, the
midget, replied, “ I guess they are daffy hen-
pickers, all the lot of them.”
The story goes that a penurious Sydney
medico some time since bought, at auction, a job-
lot of boy’s paper-collars, intending to dispose of
them at considerable profit to his very juvenile
groom. But when he got them home he found
that they would not ht. And ever since, the
story goes on to say, he has kept a sharp look-out
for a boy with a number seven neck.
A sign of the days in which we live is that
both the Sydney Sunday papers are strongly Pro-
tectionist. The reading of the sign is that the
day of Freetrade is done in N.S. Wales. No
paper ever starts on Freetrade principles now-a-
days, as papers used to of yore. Those papers
which were thus suckled in Freetrade have been
compelled by force of groove to remain so, but the
newcomer is almost invariably Protectionist.
A Pentridge prisoner who tried to hang
himself is to be punished for casting contempt on
the deterrent terrors of the Law ! We submit the
problem here indicated for the thoughtful con-
sideration of the logicians of Australia. How do
the penal authorities propose to deter the man
who wants to die ? And, assuming that he doesn’t
always want to die, but only attempts suicide
when his intellect is unusually aberrated, how do
they propose to punish him for manifesting
symptoms of insanity ?
Hear Bulletin, —-What is the derivation
of the Australian slang word “ringer?” It is
much in use amongst shearers, the “ ringer of the
shed ” being the man who makes the biggest tally
of sheep shorn, and hence the expression “he’s a
regular ringer,” has come into our everyday
vocabulary as synonymous with smartness and
agility. Carbine’s a “regular ringer ”on the
turf, Tommy Bent at land-b oming, and so on. I
am by no means sure that the word, as commonly
supposed, hailed originally from the woolshed. I
remember the expression 20 years ago amongst the
alluvial miners of the M.L. west coast. The
digger, who on panning off a prospect obtained a
circle of “colours” completely surrounding the
bottom of his dish called it a “ringer.” And the
same term may in time have began to be applied
generally as a means of expressing appreciation. —
Timi Piripi.
Sydney, September, 14th, 1888. To
Messrs. Colemane and Sons, Cootamundra. —Dear
Sirs, —It is with pleasure I take the opportunity
of writing to you, to let you know the wonderful
benefit I derived from your Eucalypte Extract.
Some time ago, if you remember, I wrote to you
from Camden, relative to a complaint of the lungs,
which at the time I was suffering from, and were
it not for your Extract I am sure I would be now
in my grave. I felt so bad that my voice was
gone, and the cough I was subject to would have
killed me, were it not for your Eucalypte Extract,
which has now, thank God, restored me to health.
I remain, yours sincerely, Patrick Carroll, 242
Hereford-street, Forest Lodge, Sydney.
The Theatre Royal Hotel, Sydney, has
lately changed hands, and is now under entirely
new management. A splendid stock of wines,
spirits, liqueurs and cigars has been laid in, and
an altogether new feature is provided in the shape
of an American bar, where all the strange, curious
and refreshing drinks known in the United States
can be obtained. Their name is legion, but among
them may be mentioned champagne cocktails,
gin fizz, milk punch, mint julep—probably the
noblest liquor known to man—imperial punch,
Manhattan cocktails, and many others. All
mixed by American bar-keeps. James A. Walsh,
proprietor, late of the Occidental Hotel, San
Francisco.*

The Melbourne police seem to have a
“soft thing on ” (and off) in poor old John Starling,
an habitual criminal who works under the dis-
advantage of a glass eye. John’s mania is for
picking pockets, and at irregular intervals during
the past twenty years he has been allowed out of
gaol that he might provide the active and intelli-
gent “coppers” with a few days’ sport. The
miserable fool invariably wanders off to a busy
thoroughfare, with his one genuine optic fixed on
the main chance, and its long-lost brother repre-
sented by a glassy glare, whilst a myrmidon of
the law shepherds him on the side where he can’t
see. In due course John’s offending paw is caught
under incriminating circumstances and the authori-
ties welcome their old friend back to gaol after
his brief airing. From a sporting point of view
this is a flagrant case of cruelty to animals. It is
much the same as hunting a three-legged hare, or
potting a partridge that has had its wings clipped.
Humanity cries aloud against any more letting of
John Starling loose upon a world where he has no
chance to follow the only trade left open to him.
He has just been sent up again for 12 months,
with a fair prospect, as his counsel pointed out, of
“ laying his poor old bones on a death-bed in the
gaol,” and if he should survive this sentence, he
ought to be promptly put out of his misery with a
{)oll-axe. Armed burglars and other violent
unatics are permitted to ravage Melbourne pretty
nearly as they please, all the time that detectives
are effecting clever captures of lame “drunks,” or
playing blind-man’s-buff with a one-eyed pick-
pocket.

Woolner, the sculptor—whose acquaint-
ance Parkes formed in Sydney in 1854 when he
was running the Empire —was very canny when
he presented New South Wales, through Premier
Parkes, with Turner’s great picture entitled
“London—Autumnal Morning ” (the picture was
exhibited by Turner at the Royal Academy of
Arts in the year before his election as an acade-
mician). The gift was intended to mark Wool-
ner’s “ remembrance of the kindness and hospi-
tality received from the inhabitants of Sydney ”
during his visit in 1854. Twenty-eight years he
took to make up his mind ! The gift proved to
be a good investment. Wi olner got numerous
commissions from Parkes, an 1 but for the advent
of the Dibbs Administration the sculptor would
now be engaged upon statues of Wentworth,
Cowper, and Martin at a cost of 3000 guineas
each to the province. Parkes has always been a
liberal patron of the fine arts —at the people’s
expense. His very latest order was for 80 statues
in stone, at ,£2OO each, to fill the niches in the
fronts of the public buildings ; .£9450 plus .£16,000
is .£25,450 worth of extravagant loan-expenditure
nipped in the bud ! Hurrah for Parkes and
“good Government ” !

Dear Bulletin, —Under “Personal Items,”
page 10, in your issue of 28th ult. appears—
The original MS. of Cary’s translation of Dante is some-
where in N.S.W., Judge Cary, a son of the translator,
having had it in his possession in Sydney.
The MS. above referred to was. I think, in the
possession of Mr, Samuel H. Smyth, late manager
of the Sydney Marine Insurance Co., of George-
street, Sydney, who was an intimate friend of the
late Judge Cary up to the time of his death, June
30, 1870. Other valuable manuscript was also
held by Mr. Smyth, but what has become of it I
know not. Judge Cary translated “ Herodotus ”
and “ Homer.” A copy of the former translation
he presented to the Public Library of Sydney
prior to his accepting the position of Chairman of
Quarter Sessions, I think in 1857 or 1858.—Yours
faithfully, Yirtute Excerpive,

that retailed by the leading West-end hosiers.

The cabled bomb-attack on Russell Sage,
the N.Y. financier, by two lunatics, is a strange
occurrence, in this way—that lunatics rarely com-
bine. Criminals combine, but not madmen, which
is the reason why so few guards are required at
asylums as compared with prisons.

Gleaming Australian Gems.
THE BEAUTY OF QUEENSLAND OPALS.
It is about ten years since opals were discovered
in Queensland, near Thargomindah, and it is
now known there is a band of “country” con-
taining these gems running right through from
“ Whitecliffs,” in New South Wales, to Cape
York, the extreme point of the first-named colony.
The gems are found mostly in “pockets,” in a
pipeclay formation, and are rarely to be found
below a depth of from 2ft, to 12ft. from the sur-
face. The best of this class of jewels comes from
“Whitecliffs,” and two very large parcels have
recently been received by Messrs. James M ‘Bean
and Son, jewellers, of Elizabeth Street, who
possess very extensive but at the same time simple
machinery for converting the rough glass-like stone
of the ground into the beautiful jewel of commerce.
The stones, having been cut and polished, are
worthy of the adornment of the proudest lady in
the land; and set in gold, as necklets or bracelets,
they are rich almost beyond compare, containing
as they do all the glittering hues of the rainbow,
and assuming a different “flash of colour” at
every movement. They are by no means of the
dull, milky, uninteresting colour commonly
ascribed to opals, but, on the contrary, promise to
be some of the most popular and attractive gems
of the day as they become better known through-
out the world. This they are sure to be as the
output becomes greater and the industry grows
larger. There used to be an absurd superstition
that opals were an “unlucky” stone, but that
idea is rapidly passing away, and will certainly
be entirely stamped out when the brilliant Aus-
tralian opal becomes better known. The most
recently polished of these now on view at the
jeweller’s above-mentioned are well worthy the
inspection of connoisseurs of such bijouterie. —
Evening Standard, 20th Nov., 1891.

THE BEALE PATENT ■
HIGH ARM SEWING MACHINES
Have, by their continued merits and the general
satisfaction they have invariably given, won the
first place in public esteem, and are on all sides
admitted to be the best and most useful sewing
machines in the market.
FOR USE IN THE HOUSEHOLD
The Beale Machine presents innumerable advant-
ages. The mechanism and the are
perfect, while the working is most simple; and
the addition of all the most recent inventions and
improvements renders the manipulation of every-
thing connected with the machine a matter, of
perfect ease. The result of this is, that with
ordinary care the machine will last for many
years, nothing can get out of cider, and no time
is wasted in tiresome meddling w ith the parts.
It is a servant that will never grumble, never
rebel, never want nights out, willing even on
washing days. It only asks for a careful cleaning
now and then, a snug, dry corner and a little
genuine sewing machine oil. Any other oil will
make it sick, and dirt of all kind it abhors.
The Self-threading Shuttle, of solid sttel,
saves the trouble of threading by the end of the
cotton. It has an excellent tension, is strong and
simple.
The Bobbin Winder is a marvel of in-
genuity, winding and guiding the cotton by
itself, as evenly as upon the reel whence it came,
and stopping of its own accord when the bobbin
is full.
For all kinds of family sewing and fancy the
Beale Machine has the greatest capacity.
The Improved High Arm. A great de-
sideratum with ladies has been the raising of the
arm of the machine, so as to allow the goods to be
sewn freely to pass underneath. This had to be
accomplished without increasing the weight of the
machine. In fact, the Beale runs with wonderful
ease and rapidity. It is almost silent, all clicking
sounds having been obviated. As is well known,
a good test of the finish of any mechanism is its
silence when in rapid motion.
With the Wonderful Beale Machine,
whether for hand or foot use, you can hem any
width, make double seams or fells, braid stitch
with chenille, Berlin wool, or gold cord, and such
like fancy work ; you may make a pretty lace
stitch, tuck ever so finely, bind dresses, coats, or
carpets ; sew on trimmings without guiding, make
frilling, ruffling, or ruching; quilt, weave your
own braid with any combination of colours and
stitch it on at the same lime; make thick, woollen
door-mats, embroidered antimacassars, window
curtains, and so forth ; you can easily and rapidly
perform the fashionable shirring or gathering so
popular for sleeves, bodies, or skirts of dresses
with an appliance supplied gratis ; lay the small
cords in hoods and stays ;do large cording; gather
with or without a band, and generally do all
kinds of straight sewing upon any material what-
ever. You can darn upon any woven goods, and
do crewel-work. The Beale Patent will pass
from eight folds of tweed or twenty of calico to
two of thinnest muslin, or even mosquito-net or
tulle, without the operator changing needle,
cotton, stitch, or tension, and the fine material
will not be gathered in the slightest. You may
make button and eyelet-holes, do over-casting,
make herring-bone stitch upon any materials.
This work is absolutely unique and can only be
accomplished upon the Beale Patent Machine,
Any of these performances will be shown at our
shops at any time. The Beale Patent makes
a lock-stitch, which cannot ravel, yet by a momen-
tary alteration the sewing can be done so as to
rip from end to end, if desired. This peculiarity
is sometimes useful when children’s clothes are
intended to be altered.
Nickel-plating. —In our climate the exposed
parts of the machine, and the applicances, need
to be protected from rust. We have, at consider-
able expense, added a handsomely nickeled wheel,
nickeled plates, screws, appliances, treadle-bar
and cones. Heightening the appearance, these
improvements also tend largely to preserve the
parts. This plating is imperishable.
A special feature of The Beale Machine is
that
ALL THE CABINET WORK ,
is made at our factory in Sydney from registered
designs, and of the choicest
AUSTRALIAN WOODS,
some of which are without doubt the most beauti-
ful in the world. Suitability to the climate is
thus insured, and we are enabled to offer a greater
variety of styles, most handsome in design and
conveniently fitted for all the requirements of a
lady’s work-table.
These machines can be inspected at any time at
our principal show-rooms in Sydney, or at any of
our suburban and country branch shops, or from
travellers who are continually visiting all parts.
All the machines may be had upon easy time-
navment, or for cash less three shillings in the
pound.
Photographs, Price-Lists and Samples
FREE BY POST.
No matter where you live, we will supply
you with a Sewing Machine upon easy Time-
Payment.

Mount Blackwood, November 9, 1891.
Gentlemen, —I hereby beg to state that, in October, 1890, I was suffering from severe pains in my right side, and also across my back. After
a little time my eyesight began to fail—especially the right eye —and became so bad that I was unable to get about the place without the assistance
of my wife. I then went to a professional oculist in Melbourne, being under him live months, but received not the least benefit from the treatment.
I next applied to the Melbourne Hospital, but was refused admission, as they could do nothing for mo. After this I returned home. (Seeing that my
water was very thick, I thought your Safe Cure might do me good. I commenced to take it about four months ago, and from the first bottle found
relief. 1 have been taking it ever since, with the result that all my pains have left me- my eyesight has so much improved that 1 can get about by
myself, and do my own gardening —my appetite is good, and I sleep well. lam quite certain 1 owe my life to your Safe Cure, and I send you this,
testimonial that others, who perhaps may be suffering as I was, may see it. My age is GO.
Witness John Cann.
